<h1 class="firstHeading mw-first-heading" id="firstHeading"><span class="mw-page-title-main">Malaria</span></h1>
<div class="vector-body" id="bodyContent">
<div class="noprint" id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
<div id="contentSub"></div>
<div id="contentSub2"></div>

id="cite_ref-who2020_4-1"><a href="#cite_note-who2020-4">[4]</a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Malaria</b> is a <a href="/wiki/Mosquito-borne_disease" title="Mosquito-borne disease">mosquito-borne infectious disease</a> that affects humans and other animals.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who.int.2022_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-who.int.2022-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> Malaria causes <a href="/wiki/Signs_and_symptoms" title="Signs and symptoms">symptoms</a> that typically include <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatigue_(medical)" title="Fatigue (medical)">tiredness</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headaches</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cara2014_1-5"><a href="#cite_note-Cara2014-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> In severe cases, it can cause <a href="/wiki/Jaundice" title="Jaundice">jaundice</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Epileptic_seizure" title="Epileptic seizure">seizures</a>, <a href="/wiki/Coma" title="Coma">coma</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">death</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cara2014_1-6"><a href="#cite_note-Cara2014-1">[1]</a></sup> Symptoms usually begin ten to fifteen days after being bitten by an infected <a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">mosquito</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> If not properly treated, people may have recurrences of the disease months later.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-4"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> In those who have recently survived an infection, reinfection usually causes milder symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cara2014_1-7"><a href="#cite_note-Cara2014-1">[1]</a></sup> This partial <a href="/wiki/Immunity_(medical)" title="Immunity (medical)">resistance</a> disappears over months to years if the person has no continuing exposure to malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cara2014_1-8"><a href="#cite_note-Cara2014-1">[1]</a></sup>
</p><p>Malaria is caused by <a href="/wiki/Protozoa" title="Protozoa">single-celled microorganisms</a> of the <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium" title="Plasmodium">Plasmodium</a></i> group.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-5"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> It is spread exclusively through bites of infected <i><a href="/wiki/Anopheles" title="Anopheles">Anopheles</a></i> mosquitoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-6"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> The mosquito bite introduces the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a> from the mosquito's <a href="/wiki/Saliva" title="Saliva">saliva</a> into a person's blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-7"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> The parasites travel to the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> where they mature and reproduce.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cara2014_1-9"><a href="#cite_note-Cara2014-1">[1]</a></sup> Five species of <i>Plasmodium</i> can infect and be spread by humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cara2014_1-10"><a href="#cite_note-Cara2014-1">[1]</a></sup> Most deaths are caused by <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_falciparum" title="Plasmodium falciparum">P. falciparum</a></i>, whereas <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_vivax" title="Plasmodium vivax">P. vivax</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_ovale" title="Plasmodium ovale">P. ovale</a></i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_malariae" title="Plasmodium malariae">P. malariae</a></i> generally cause a milder form of malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cara2014_1-11"><a href="#cite_note-Cara2014-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-8"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> The species <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_knowlesi" title="Plasmodium knowlesi">P. knowlesi</a></i> rarely causes disease in humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-9"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> Malaria is typically diagnosed by the <a href="/wiki/Histology" title="Histology">microscopic examination</a> of blood using <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_film" title="Blood film">blood films</a>, or with <a href="/wiki/Malaria_antigen_detection_tests" title="Malaria antigen detection tests">antigen-based</a> <a href="/wiki/Rapid_diagnostic_test" title="Rapid diagnostic test">rapid diagnostic tests</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cara2014_1-12"><a href="#cite_note-Cara2014-1">[1]</a></sup> Methods that use the <a href="/wiki/Polymerase_chain_reaction" title="Polymerase chain reaction">polymerase chain reaction</a> to detect the parasite's <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> have been developed, but are not widely used in areas where malaria is common due to their cost and complexity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadjm_2012_9-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nadjm_2012-9">[9]</a></sup>
</p><p>The risk of disease can be reduced by preventing mosquito bites through the use of <a href="/wiki/Mosquito_net" title="Mosquito net">mosquito nets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Insect_repellent" title="Insect repellent">insect repellents</a> or with <a href="/wiki/Mosquito_control" title="Mosquito control">mosquito-control measures</a> such as spraying <a href="/wiki/Insecticide" title="Insecticide">insecticides</a> and draining <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Standing_water" title="Standing water">standing water</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cara2014_1-13"><a href="#cite_note-Cara2014-1">[1]</a></sup> Several medications are available to <a href="/wiki/Malaria_prophylaxis" title="Malaria prophylaxis">prevent malaria</a> for travellers in areas where the disease is common.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-10"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> Occasional doses of the combination medication <a href="/wiki/Sulfadoxine/pyrimethamine" title="Sulfadoxine/pyrimethamine">sulfadoxine/pyrimethamine</a> are recommended in infants and after the first trimester of pregnancy in areas with high rates of malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-11"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> As of 2020, there is one <a href="/wiki/Malaria_vaccine" title="Malaria vaccine">vaccine</a> which has been shown to reduce the risk of malaria by about 40% in children in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2020Fact_10-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2020Fact-10">[10]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016-11">[11]</a></sup> A pre-print study of another vaccine has shown 77% vaccine efficacy, but this study has not yet passed peer review.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Dates_and_numbers#Chronological_items" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Dates and numbers"><span title="The date of the event predicted near this tag has passed. (June 2022)">needs update</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> Efforts to develop more effective vaccines are ongoing.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016_11-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016-11">[11]</a></sup> The recommended treatment for malaria is a <a href="/wiki/Antimalarial_medication#Artemisinin-based_combination_therapies_(ACTs)" title="Antimalarial medication">combination</a> of <a href="/wiki/Antimalarial_medication" title="Antimalarial medication">antimalarial medications</a> that includes <a href="/wiki/Artemisinin" title="Artemisinin">artemisinin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:5_13-0"><a href="#cite_note-:5-13">[13]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cara2014_1-14"><a href="#cite_note-Cara2014-1">[1]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-12"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> The second medication may be either <a href="/wiki/Mefloquine" title="Mefloquine">mefloquine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lumefantrine" title="Lumefantrine">lumefantrine</a>, or sulfadoxine/pyrimethamine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2010_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2010-15">[15]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Quinine" title="Quinine">Quinine</a>, along with <a href="/wiki/Doxycycline" title="Doxycycline">doxycycline</a>, may be used if artemisinin is not available.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2010_15-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2010-15">[15]</a></sup> It is recommended that in areas where the disease is common, malaria is confirmed if possible before treatment is started due to concerns of increasing <a href="/wiki/Drug_resistance" title="Drug resistance">drug resistance</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-13"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> Resistance among the parasites has developed to several antimalarial medications; for example, <a href="/wiki/Chloroquine" title="Chloroquine">chloroquine</a>-resistant <i>P. falciparum</i> has spread to most malarial areas, and resistance to artemisinin has become a problem in some parts of Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-14"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup>
</p><p>The disease is widespread in the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical" title="Subtropical">subtropical</a> regions that exist in a broad band around the <a href="/wiki/Equator" title="Equator">equator</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cara2014_1-15"><a href="#cite_note-Cara2014-1">[1]</a></sup> This includes much of <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">sub-Saharan Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Latin_America" title="Latin America">Latin America</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-15"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> In 2020 there were 241 million cases of malaria worldwide resulting in an estimated 627,000 deaths. Approximately 95% of the cases and deaths occurred in sub-Saharan Africa. Rates of disease have decreased from 2010 to 2014 but increased from 2015 to 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2020_4-2"><a href="#cite_note-who2020-4">[4]</a></sup> Malaria is commonly associated with poverty and has a significant negative effect on economic development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IftSoL_17-0"><a href="#cite_note-IftSoL-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worrall_2005_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-Worrall_2005-18">[18]</a></sup> In Africa, it is estimated to result in losses of US$12 billion a year due to increased healthcare costs, lost ability to work, and adverse effects on tourism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_2005_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood_2005-19">[19]</a></sup>
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Signs_and_symptoms">Signs and symptoms</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Signs and symptoms">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_Malaria.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1785" data-file-width="1683" decoding="async" height="265" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Symptoms_of_Malaria.png/250px-Symptoms_of_Malaria.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Symptoms_of_Malaria.png/375px-Symptoms_of_Malaria.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/db/Symptoms_of_Malaria.png/500px-Symptoms_of_Malaria.png 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Symptoms_of_Malaria.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Main symptoms of malaria<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PPID_2010_20-0"><a href="#cite_note-PPID_2010-20">[20]</a></sup></div></div></div>
<p>Adults with malaria tend to experience chills and fever – classically in periodic <a href="/wiki/Paroxysmal_attack" title="Paroxysmal attack">intense bouts</a> lasting around six hours, followed by a period of sweating and fever relief – as well as headache, fatigue, abdominal discomfort, and <a href="/wiki/Myalgia" title="Myalgia">muscle pain</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PD7_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-PD7-21">[21]</a></sup> Children tend to have more general symptoms: fever, cough, vomiting, and diarrhea.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PD7_21-1"><a href="#cite_note-PD7-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Initial manifestations of the disease—common to all malaria species—are similar to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Flu" title="Flu">flu-like symptoms</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartoloni_2012_22-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bartoloni_2012-22">[22]</a></sup> and can resemble other conditions such as <a href="/wiki/Sepsis" title="Sepsis">sepsis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Gastroenteritis" title="Gastroenteritis">gastroenteritis</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Viral_disease" title="Viral disease">viral diseases</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadjm_2012_9-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nadjm_2012-9">[9]</a></sup> The presentation may include <a href="/wiki/Headache" title="Headache">headache</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fever" title="Fever">fever</a>, <a href="/wiki/Shivering" title="Shivering">shivering</a>, <a href="/wiki/Arthralgia" title="Arthralgia">joint pain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vomiting" title="Vomiting">vomiting</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemolytic_anemia" title="Hemolytic anemia">hemolytic anemia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jaundice" title="Jaundice">jaundice</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobinuria" title="Hemoglobinuria">hemoglobin in the urine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Retinopathy" title="Retinopathy">retinal damage</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Convulsion" title="Convulsion">convulsions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beare_2006_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beare_2006-23">[23]</a></sup>
</p><p>The classic symptom of malaria is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paroxysmal_attacks" title="Paroxysmal attacks">paroxysm</a>—a cyclical occurrence of sudden coldness followed by shivering and then fever and sweating, occurring every two days (<a href="/wiki/Fever#Types" title="Fever">tertian fever</a>) in <i>P. vivax</i> and <i>P. ovale</i> infections, and every three days (<a href="/wiki/Fever#Types" title="Fever">quartan fever</a>) for <i>P. malariae</i>. <i>P. falciparum</i> infection can cause recurrent fever every 36–48 hours, or a less pronounced and almost continuous fever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ferri_2009_24-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ferri_2009-24">[24]</a></sup>
</p><p>Symptoms typically begin 10–15 days after the initial mosquito bite, but can occur as late as several months after infection with some <i>P. vivax</i> strains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PD7_21-2"><a href="#cite_note-PD7-21">[21]</a></sup> Travellers taking preventative malaria medications may develop symptoms once they stop taking the drugs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-PD7_21-3"><a href="#cite_note-PD7-21">[21]</a></sup>
</p><p>Severe malaria is usually caused by <i>P. falciparum</i> (often referred to as falciparum malaria). Symptoms of falciparum malaria arise 9–30 days after infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartoloni_2012_22-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bartoloni_2012-22">[22]</a></sup> Individuals with cerebral malaria frequently exhibit <a href="/wiki/Neurological_disorder" title="Neurological disorder">neurological</a> symptoms, including <a href="/wiki/Abnormal_posturing" title="Abnormal posturing">abnormal posturing</a>, <a href="/wiki/Nystagmus" title="Nystagmus">nystagmus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Conjugate_gaze_palsy" title="Conjugate gaze palsy">conjugate gaze palsy</a> (failure of the eyes to turn together in the same direction), <a href="/wiki/Opisthotonus" title="Opisthotonus">opisthotonus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Seizure" title="Seizure">seizures</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Coma" title="Coma">coma</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartoloni_2012_22-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bartoloni_2012-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Complications">Complications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Complications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Malaria has several serious <a href="/wiki/Complication_(medicine)" title="Complication (medicine)">complications</a>. Among these is the development of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Respiratory_distress" title="Respiratory distress">respiratory distress</a>, which occurs in up to 25% of adults and 40% of children with severe <i>P. falciparum</i> malaria. Possible causes include respiratory compensation of <a href="/wiki/Metabolic_acidosis" title="Metabolic acidosis">metabolic acidosis</a>, noncardiogenic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary_oedema" title="Pulmonary oedema">pulmonary oedema</a>, concomitant <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>, and severe <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaemia" title="Anaemia">anaemia</a>. Although rare in young children with severe malaria, <a href="/wiki/Acute_respiratory_distress_syndrome" title="Acute respiratory distress syndrome">acute respiratory distress syndrome</a> occurs in 5–25% of adults and up to 29% of pregnant women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_2012_25-0"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor_2012-25">[25]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Coinfection" title="Coinfection">Coinfection</a> of <a href="/wiki/HIV" title="HIV">HIV</a> with malaria increases mortality.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Korenromp_2005_26-0"><a href="#cite_note-Korenromp_2005-26">[26]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Kidney_failure" title="Kidney failure">Kidney failure</a> is a feature of <a href="/wiki/Blackwater_fever" title="Blackwater fever">blackwater fever</a>, where haemoglobin from <a href="/wiki/Lysis" title="Lysis">lysed</a> red blood cells leaks into the urine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartoloni_2012_22-3"><a href="#cite_note-Bartoloni_2012-22">[22]</a></sup>
</p><p>Infection with <i>P. falciparum</i> may result in cerebral malaria, a form of severe malaria that involves <a href="/wiki/Encephalopathy" title="Encephalopathy">encephalopathy</a>. It is associated with retinal whitening, which may be a useful clinical sign in distinguishing malaria from other causes of fever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Beare_2011_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-Beare_2011-27">[27]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Splenomegaly" title="Splenomegaly">enlarged spleen</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hepatomegaly" title="Hepatomegaly">enlarged liver</a> or <a href="/wiki/Hepatosplenomegaly" title="Hepatosplenomegaly">both of these</a>, severe headache, <a href="/wiki/Hypoglycemia" title="Hypoglycemia">low blood sugar</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobinuria" title="Hemoglobinuria">haemoglobin in the urine</a> with <a href="/wiki/Kidney_failure" title="Kidney failure">kidney failure</a> may occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bartoloni_2012_22-4"><a href="#cite_note-Bartoloni_2012-22">[22]</a></sup> Complications may include spontaneous bleeding, <a href="/wiki/Coagulopathy" title="Coagulopathy">coagulopathy</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circulatory_shock" title="Circulatory shock">shock</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Pregnancy-associated_malaria" title="Pregnancy-associated malaria">Malaria in pregnant women</a> is an important cause of <a href="/wiki/Stillbirth" title="Stillbirth">stillbirths</a>, <a href="/wiki/Infant_mortality" title="Infant mortality">infant mortality</a>, <a href="/wiki/Miscarriage" title="Miscarriage">miscarriage</a> and <a href="/wiki/Low_birth_weight" title="Low birth weight">low birth weight</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartman_2010_29-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hartman_2010-29">[29]</a></sup> particularly in <i>P. falciparum</i> infection, but also with <i>P. vivax</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rijken_2012_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rijken_2012-30">[30]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cause">Cause</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Cause">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Malaria is caused by infection with <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parasite" title="Parasite">parasites</a> in the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium" title="Plasmodium">Plasmodium</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2_31-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2-31">[31]</a></sup> In humans, malaria is caused by six <i>Plasmodium</i> species: <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_falciparum" title="Plasmodium falciparum">P. falciparum</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_malariae" title="Plasmodium malariae">P. malariae</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_ovale_curtisi" title="Plasmodium ovale curtisi">P. ovale curtisi</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_ovale_wallikeri" title="Plasmodium ovale wallikeri">P. ovale wallikeri</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_vivax" title="Plasmodium vivax">P. vivax</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_knowlesi" title="Plasmodium knowlesi">P. knowlesi</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ashley2018_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ashley2018-32">[32]</a></sup> Among those infected, <i>P. falciparum</i> is the most common species identified (~75%) followed by <i>P. vivax</i> (~20%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadjm_2012_9-2"><a href="#cite_note-Nadjm_2012-9">[9]</a></sup> Although <i>P. falciparum</i> traditionally accounts for the majority of deaths,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar_2009_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar_2009-33">[33]</a></sup> recent evidence suggests that <i>P. vivax</i> malaria is associated with potentially life-threatening conditions about as often as with a diagnosis of <i>P. falciparum</i> infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baird_2013_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baird_2013-34">[34]</a></sup> <i>P. vivax</i> proportionally is more common outside Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arnott_2012_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arnott_2012-35">[35]</a></sup> There have been documented human infections with several species of <i>Plasmodium</i> from <a href="/wiki/Simian" title="Simian">higher apes</a>; however, except for <i>P. knowlesi</i>—a <a href="/wiki/Zoonosis" title="Zoonosis">zoonotic</a> species that causes malaria in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Macaques" title="Macaques">macaques</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_2012_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins_2012-36">[36]</a></sup>—these are mostly of limited public health importance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Collins_2009_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-Collins_2009-37">[37]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:352px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Life_Cycle_of_the_Malaria_Parasite.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1282" data-file-width="1300" decoding="async" height="345" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Life_Cycle_of_the_Malaria_Parasite.jpg/350px-Life_Cycle_of_the_Malaria_Parasite.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Life_Cycle_of_the_Malaria_Parasite.jpg/525px-Life_Cycle_of_the_Malaria_Parasite.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9a/Life_Cycle_of_the_Malaria_Parasite.jpg/700px-Life_Cycle_of_the_Malaria_Parasite.jpg 2x" width="350"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Life_Cycle_of_the_Malaria_Parasite.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The life cycle of malaria parasites. Sporozoites are introduced by a mosquito bite. They migrate to the liver, where they multiply into thousands of merozoites. The merozoites infect red blood cells and replicate, infecting more and more red blood cells. Some parasites form gametocytes, which are taken up by a mosquito, continuing the life cycle.</div></div></div>
<p>Parasites are typically introduced by the bite of an infected <i><a href="/wiki/Anopheles" title="Anopheles">Anopheles</a></i> mosquito. What these inoculated parasites, called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sporozoite" title="Sporozoite">sporozoites</a>", do in the skin and lymphatics, exactly, has yet to be accurately determined.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-38"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup> However, a percentage of sporozoites follow the bloodstream to the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a>, where they invade <a href="/wiki/Hepatocyte" title="Hepatocyte">hepatocytes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowman2016_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cowman2016-39">[39]</a></sup> They grow and divide in the liver for 2–10 days, with each infected hepatocyte eventually harboring up to 40,000 parasites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowman2016_39-1"><a href="#cite_note-Cowman2016-39">[39]</a></sup> The infected hepatocytes break down, releasing this invasive form of <i>Plasmodium</i> cells, called "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Merozoite" title="Merozoite">merozoites</a>" into the bloodstream. In the blood, the merozoites rapidly invade individual <a href="/wiki/Red_blood_cell" title="Red blood cell">red blood cells</a>, replicating over 24–72 hours to form 16–32 new merozoites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowman2016_39-2"><a href="#cite_note-Cowman2016-39">[39]</a></sup> The infected red blood cell lyses, and the new merozoites infect new red blood cells, resulting in a cycle that continuously amplifies the number of parasites in an infected person.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowman2016_39-3"><a href="#cite_note-Cowman2016-39">[39]</a></sup> However, most of the <i>P. vivax</i> replicating merozoite biomass is now (since 2021) known to be hidden in the spleen and bone marrow (perhaps elsewhere too), thereby supporting the astute, long-standing (since 2011) but previously ignored theory that non-circulating merozoites are the source many <i>P. vivax</i> malarial recurrences (see “Recurrent malaria” section below).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> Over rounds of this red blood cell infection cycle in the bloodstream and elsewhere, a small portion of parasites do not replicate, but instead develop into early sexual stage parasites called male and female "<a href="/wiki/Gametocyte" title="Gametocyte">gametocytes</a>". These gametocytes develop in the <a href="/wiki/Bone_marrow" title="Bone marrow">bone marrow</a> for 11 days, then return to the blood circulation to await uptake by the bite of another mosquito.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowman2016_39-4"><a href="#cite_note-Cowman2016-39">[39]</a></sup> Once inside a mosquito, the gametocytes undergo sexual reproduction, and eventually form daughter sporozoites that migrate to the mosquito's <a href="/wiki/Salivary_gland" title="Salivary gland">salivary glands</a> to be injected into a new host when the mosquito bites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cowman2016_39-5"><a href="#cite_note-Cowman2016-39">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>The liver infection causes no symptoms; all symptoms of malaria result from the infection of red blood cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ashley2018_32-1"><a href="#cite_note-Ashley2018-32">[32]</a></sup> Symptoms develop once there are more than around 100,000 parasites per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Milliliter" title="Milliliter">milliliter</a> of blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ashley2018_32-2"><a href="#cite_note-Ashley2018-32">[32]</a></sup> Many of the symptoms associated with severe malaria are caused by the tendency of <i>P. falciparum</i> to bind to <a href="/wiki/Blood_vessel" title="Blood vessel">blood vessel</a> walls, resulting in damage to the affected vessels and surrounding tissue. Parasites sequestered in the blood vessels of the lung contribute to <a href="/wiki/Respiratory_failure" title="Respiratory failure">respiratory failure</a>. In the brain, they contribute to <a href="/wiki/Coma" title="Coma">coma</a>. In the placenta they contribute to low birthweight and preterm labor, and increase the risk of abortion and stillbirth.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ashley2018_32-3"><a href="#cite_note-Ashley2018-32">[32]</a></sup> The destruction of red blood cells during infection often results in anemia, exacerbated by reduced production of new red blood cells during infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ashley2018_32-4"><a href="#cite_note-Ashley2018-32">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>Only female mosquitoes feed on blood; male mosquitoes feed on plant nectar and do not transmit the disease. Females of the mosquito genus <i>Anopheles</i> prefer to feed at night. They usually start searching for a meal at dusk, and continue through the night until they succeed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Arrow_2004_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-Arrow_2004-41">[41]</a></sup> Malaria parasites can also be transmitted by <a href="/wiki/Blood_transfusion" title="Blood transfusion">blood transfusions</a>, although this is rare.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Owusu-Ofori_2010_42-0"><a href="#cite_note-Owusu-Ofori_2010-42">[42]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Recurrent_malaria">Recurrent malaria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Recurrent malaria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Symptoms of malaria can recur after varying symptom-free periods. Depending upon the cause, recurrence can be classified as <a href="/wiki/Recrudescence" title="Recrudescence">recrudescence</a>, <a href="/wiki/Relapse" title="Relapse">relapse</a>, or reinfection. Recrudescence is when symptoms return after a symptom-free period and the origin is parasites that survived in the blood as a result of inadequate or ineffective treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> Relapse is when symptoms reappear after the parasites have been eliminated from the blood and the recurrence source is activated parasites which had persisted as dormant <a href="/wiki/Plasmodium#Life_cycle" title="Plasmodium">hypnozoites</a> in liver cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Relapse commonly occurs after 8–24 weeks and is often seen in <i>P. vivax</i> and <i>P. ovale</i> infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadjm_2012_9-3"><a href="#cite_note-Nadjm_2012-9">[9]</a></sup> However, relapse-like <i>P. vivax</i> recurrences are probably being over-attributed to hypnozoite activation. Some of them might have an extra-vascular or sequestered merozoite origin, making those recurrences recrudescences, not relapses.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Newly recognised, non-hypnozoite, possible contributing sources to recurrent peripheral <i>P. vivax</i> parasitemia are erythrocytic forms in the bone marrow and spleen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-46"><a href="#cite_note-46">[46]</a></sup> <i>P. vivax</i> malaria cases in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Temperate" title="Temperate">temperate</a> areas often involve <a href="/wiki/Overwintering" title="Overwintering">overwintering</a> by hypnozoites, with relapses beginning the year after the mosquito bite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White_2011_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-White_2011-47">[47]</a></sup> Reinfection means that the parasites responsible for the past infection were eliminated from the body but a new parasite(s) was introduced. Reinfection cannot readily be distinguished from relapse and recrudescence, although recurrence of infection within two weeks of treatment for the initial malarial manifestations is typically attributed to treatment failure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-48"><a href="#cite_note-48">[48]</a></sup> But doing this is not necessarily correct.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup> People may develop some <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Premunity" title="Premunity">immunity</a> when exposed to frequent infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tran_2012_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tran_2012-50">[50]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pathophysiology">Pathophysiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Pathophysiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_falciparum#Pathogenesis" title="Plasmodium falciparum">Plasmodium falciparum § Pathogenesis</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Maternal_malaria_placenta_-_cropped_-_very_high_mag.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2136" data-file-width="2848" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Maternal_malaria_placenta_-_cropped_-_very_high_mag.jpg/220px-Maternal_malaria_placenta_-_cropped_-_very_high_mag.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Maternal_malaria_placenta_-_cropped_-_very_high_mag.jpg/330px-Maternal_malaria_placenta_-_cropped_-_very_high_mag.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Maternal_malaria_placenta_-_cropped_-_very_high_mag.jpg/440px-Maternal_malaria_placenta_-_cropped_-_very_high_mag.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Maternal_malaria_placenta_-_cropped_-_very_high_mag.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Micrograph" title="Micrograph">Micrograph</a> of a <a href="/wiki/Placenta" title="Placenta">placenta</a> from a <a href="/wiki/Stillbirth" title="Stillbirth">stillbirth</a> due to maternal malaria. <a href="/wiki/H%26E_stain" title="H&amp;E stain">H&amp;E stain</a>. Red blood cells are anuclear; blue/black staining in bright red structures (red blood cells) indicate foreign nuclei from the parasites.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Red_blood_cells_infected_with_malaria.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1229" data-file-width="1551" decoding="async" height="174" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Red_blood_cells_infected_with_malaria.jpg/220px-Red_blood_cells_infected_with_malaria.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Red_blood_cells_infected_with_malaria.jpg/330px-Red_blood_cells_infected_with_malaria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5b/Red_blood_cells_infected_with_malaria.jpg/440px-Red_blood_cells_infected_with_malaria.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Red_blood_cells_infected_with_malaria.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Electron micrograph of a <i>Plasmodium falciparum</i>-infected red blood cell (center), illustrating adhesion protein "knobs"</div></div></div>
<p>Malaria infection develops via two phases: one that involves the <a href="/wiki/Liver" title="Liver">liver</a> (exoerythrocytic phase), and one that involves red blood cells, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Erythrocyte" title="Erythrocyte">erythrocytes</a> (erythrocytic phase). When an infected mosquito pierces a person's skin to take a blood meal, sporozoites in the mosquito's saliva enter the bloodstream and migrate to the liver where they infect hepatocytes, multiplying asexually and asymptomatically for a period of 8–30 days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bledsoe_2005_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bledsoe_2005-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>After a potential dormant period in the liver, these organisms <a href="/wiki/Cellular_differentiation" title="Cellular differentiation">differentiate</a> to yield thousands of merozoites, which, following rupture of their host cells, escape into the blood and infect red blood cells to begin the erythrocytic stage of the life cycle.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bledsoe_2005_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bledsoe_2005-51">[51]</a></sup> The parasite escapes from the liver undetected by wrapping itself in the <a href="/wiki/Cell_membrane" title="Cell membrane">cell membrane</a> of the infected host liver cell.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vaughan_2008_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vaughan_2008-52">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>Within the red blood cells, the parasites multiply further, again asexually, periodically breaking out of their host cells to invade fresh red blood cells. Several such amplification cycles occur. Thus, classical descriptions of waves of fever arise from simultaneous waves of merozoites escaping and infecting red blood cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bledsoe_2005_51-2"><a href="#cite_note-Bledsoe_2005-51">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>Some <i>P. vivax</i> sporozoites do not immediately develop into exoerythrocytic-phase merozoites, but instead, produce hypnozoites that remain dormant for periods ranging from several months (7–10 months is typical) to several years.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White_2011_47-1"><a href="#cite_note-White_2011-47">[47]</a></sup> After a period of dormancy, they reactivate and produce merozoites. Hypnozoites are responsible for long incubation and late relapses in <i>P. vivax</i> infections,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White_2011_47-2"><a href="#cite_note-White_2011-47">[47]</a></sup> although their existence in <i>P. ovale</i> is uncertain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Richter_2010_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-Richter_2010-53">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>The parasite is relatively protected from attack by the body's <a href="/wiki/Immune_system" title="Immune system">immune system</a> because for most of its human life cycle it resides within the liver and blood cells and is relatively invisible to immune surveillance. However, circulating infected blood cells are destroyed in the <a href="/wiki/Spleen" title="Spleen">spleen</a>. To avoid this fate, the <i>P. falciparum</i> parasite displays adhesive <a href="/wiki/Protein" title="Protein">proteins</a> on the surface of the infected blood cells, causing the blood cells to stick to the walls of small blood vessels, thereby sequestering the parasite from passage through the general circulation and the spleen.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tilley_2011_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tilley_2011-54">[54]</a></sup> The blockage of the microvasculature causes symptoms such as those in placental malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mens_2010_55-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mens_2010-55">[55]</a></sup> Sequestered red blood cells can breach the <a href="/wiki/Blood%E2%80%93brain_barrier" title="Blood–brain barrier">blood–brain barrier</a> and cause cerebral malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rénia_2012_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rénia_2012-56">[56]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Genetic_resistance">Genetic resistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Genetic resistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Human_genetic_resistance_to_malaria" title="Human genetic resistance to malaria">Human genetic resistance to malaria</a></div>
<p>According to a 2005 review, due to the high levels of <a href="/wiki/Death" title="Death">mortality</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Morbidity" title="Morbidity">morbidity</a> caused by malaria—especially the <i>P. falciparum</i> species—it has placed the greatest <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Selection_(biology)" title="Selection (biology)">selective pressure</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Human_genome" title="Human genome">human genome</a> in recent history. Several genetic factors provide some resistance to it including <a href="/wiki/Sickle_cell_trait" title="Sickle cell trait">sickle cell trait</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thalassaemia" title="Thalassaemia">thalassaemia</a> traits, <a href="/wiki/Glucose-6-phosphate_dehydrogenase_deficiency" title="Glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase deficiency">glucose-6-phosphate dehydrogenase deficiency</a>, and the absence of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Duffy_antigen" title="Duffy antigen">Duffy antigens</a> on red blood cells.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kwiatkowski_2005_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kwiatkowski_2005-58">[58]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hedrick_2011_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hedrick_2011-59">[59]</a></sup>
</p><p>The impact of sickle cell trait on malaria immunity illustrates some evolutionary trade-offs that have occurred because of endemic malaria. Sickle cell trait causes a change in the haemoglobin molecule in the blood. Normally, red blood cells have a very flexible, biconcave shape that allows them to move through narrow <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Capillaries" title="Capillaries">capillaries</a>; however, when the modified <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hemoglobin_S" title="Hemoglobin S">haemoglobin S</a> molecules are exposed to low amounts of oxygen, or crowd together due to dehydration, they can stick together forming strands that cause the cell to distort into a curved sickle shape. In these strands, the molecule is not as effective in taking or releasing oxygen, and the cell is not flexible enough to circulate freely. In the early stages of malaria, the parasite can cause infected red cells to sickle, and so they are removed from circulation sooner. This reduces the frequency with which malaria parasites complete their life cycle in the cell. Individuals who are <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Homozygous" title="Homozygous">homozygous</a> (with two copies of the abnormal haemoglobin beta <a href="/wiki/Allele" title="Allele">allele</a>) have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sickle-cell_anaemia" title="Sickle-cell anaemia">sickle-cell anaemia</a>, while those who are heterozygous (with one abnormal allele and one normal allele) experience resistance to malaria without severe anaemia. Although the shorter life expectancy for those with the homozygous condition would tend to disfavour the trait's survival, the trait is preserved in malaria-prone regions because of the <a href="/wiki/Heterozygote_advantage" title="Heterozygote advantage">benefits</a> provided by the heterozygous form.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hedrick_2011_59-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hedrick_2011-59">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Weatherall_2008_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-Weatherall_2008-60">[60]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Liver_dysfunction">Liver dysfunction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Liver dysfunction">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Liver dysfunction as a result of malaria is uncommon and usually only occurs in those with another liver condition such as <a href="/wiki/Viral_hepatitis" title="Viral hepatitis">viral hepatitis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Chronic_liver_disease" title="Chronic liver disease">chronic liver disease</a>. The syndrome is sometimes called <i>malarial hepatitis</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhalla_2006_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bhalla_2006-61">[61]</a></sup> While it has been considered a rare occurrence, malarial hepatopathy has seen an increase, particularly in Southeast Asia and India. Liver compromise in people with malaria correlates with a greater likelihood of complications and death.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bhalla_2006_61-1"><a href="#cite_note-Bhalla_2006-61">[61]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Diagnosis">Diagnosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Diagnosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Diagnosis_of_malaria" title="Diagnosis of malaria">Diagnosis of malaria</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:5901_lores.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="357" data-file-width="615" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/5901_lores.jpg/220px-5901_lores.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/5901_lores.jpg/330px-5901_lores.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/5901_lores.jpg/440px-5901_lores.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:5901_lores.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The blood film is the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard_(test)" title="Gold standard (test)">gold standard</a> for malaria diagnosis.</div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Plasmodium.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="473" data-file-width="440" decoding="async" height="237" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Plasmodium.jpg/220px-Plasmodium.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3c/Plasmodium.jpg/330px-Plasmodium.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/3/3c/Plasmodium.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Plasmodium.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ring-forms and <a href="/wiki/Gametocyte" title="Gametocyte">gametocytes</a> of <i>Plasmodium falciparum</i> in human blood</div></div></div>
<p>Due to the non-specific nature of malaria symptoms, diagnosis is typically suspected based on symptoms and travel history, then confirmed with a parasitological test. In areas where malaria is common, the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) recommends clinicians suspect malaria in any person who reports having fevers, or who has a current temperature above 37.5 °C without any other obvious cause.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO-Diag_62-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-Diag-62">[62]</a></sup> Malaria should similarly be suspected in children with signs of <a href="/wiki/Anemia" title="Anemia">anemia</a>: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palmar_pallor" title="Palmar pallor">pale palms</a> or a laboratory test showing <a href="/wiki/Hemoglobin" title="Hemoglobin">hemoglobin</a> levels below 8 <a href="/wiki/Gram" title="Gram">grams</a> per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Deciliter" title="Deciliter">deciliter</a> of blood.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO-Diag_62-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-Diag-62">[62]</a></sup> In areas with little to no malaria, the WHO recommends only testing people with possible exposure to malaria (typically travel to a malaria-endemic area) and unexplained fever.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO-Diag_62-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-Diag-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>Malaria is usually confirmed by the microscopic examination of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_film" title="Blood film">blood films</a> or by <a href="/wiki/Antigen" title="Antigen">antigen</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Malaria_antigen_detection_tests" title="Malaria antigen detection tests">rapid diagnostic tests</a> (RDT). Microscopy – i.e. examining <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Giemsa" title="Giemsa">Giemsa</a>-stained blood with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Light_microscope" title="Light microscope">light microscope</a> – is the <a href="/wiki/Gold_standard_(test)" title="Gold standard (test)">gold standard</a> for malaria diagnosis.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ashley2018_32-5"><a href="#cite_note-Ashley2018-32">[32]</a></sup> Microscopists typically examine both a "thick film" of blood, allowing them to scan many blood cells in a short time, and a "thin film" of blood, allowing them to clearly see individual parasites and identify the infecting <i>Plasmodium</i> species.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ashley2018_32-6"><a href="#cite_note-Ashley2018-32">[32]</a></sup> Under typical field laboratory conditions, a microscopist can detect parasites when there are at least 100 parasites per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microliter" title="Microliter">microliter</a> of blood, which is around the lower range of symptomatic infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO-Diag_62-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-Diag-62">[62]</a></sup> Microscopic diagnosis is relatively resource intensive, requiring trained personnel, specific equipment, electricity, and a consistent supply of <a href="/wiki/Microscope_slide" title="Microscope slide">microscopy slides</a> and stains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO-Diag_62-4"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-Diag-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p>In places where microscopy is unavailable, malaria is diagnosed with RDTs, <a href="/wiki/Rapid_antigen_test" title="Rapid antigen test">rapid antigen tests</a> that detect parasite proteins in a <a href="/wiki/Fingerstick" title="Fingerstick">fingerstick</a> blood sample.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO-Diag_62-5"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-Diag-62">[62]</a></sup> A variety of RDTs are commercially available, targeting the parasite proteins <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Histidine_rich_protein_2&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Histidine rich protein 2 (page does not exist)">histidine rich protein 2</a> (HRP2, detects <i>P. falciparum</i> only), <a href="/wiki/Lactate_dehydrogenase" title="Lactate dehydrogenase">lactate dehydrogenase</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Aldolase" title="Aldolase">aldolase</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO-Diag_62-6"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-Diag-62">[62]</a></sup> The HRP2 test is widely used in Africa, where <i>P. falciparum</i> predominates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ashley2018_32-7"><a href="#cite_note-Ashley2018-32">[32]</a></sup> However, since HRP2 persists in the blood for up to five weeks after an infection is treated, an HRP2 test sometimes cannot distinguish whether someone currently has malaria or previously had it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO-Diag_62-7"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-Diag-62">[62]</a></sup> Additionally, some <i>P. falciparum</i> parasites in the Amazon region lack the <i>HRP2</i> gene, complicating detection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO-Diag_62-8"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-Diag-62">[62]</a></sup> RDTs are fast and easily deployed to places without full diagnostic laboratories.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO-Diag_62-9"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-Diag-62">[62]</a></sup> However they give considerably less information than microscopy, and sometimes vary in quality from producer to producer and lot to lot.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO-Diag_62-10"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-Diag-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Serological_test" title="Serological test">Serological tests</a> to detect antibodies against <i>Plasmodium</i> from the blood have been developed, but are not used for malaria diagnosis due to their relatively poor sensitivity and specificity. Highly sensitive <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nucleic_acid_amplification_test" title="Nucleic acid amplification test">nucleic acid amplification tests</a> have been developed, but are not used clinically due to their relatively high cost, and poor specificity for active infections.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO-Diag_62-11"><a href="#cite_note-WHO-Diag-62">[62]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Classification">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Malaria is classified into either "severe" or "uncomplicated" by the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadjm_2012_9-4"><a href="#cite_note-Nadjm_2012-9">[9]</a></sup> It is deemed severe when <i>any</i> of the following criteria are present, otherwise it is considered uncomplicated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>Decreased consciousness</li>
<li>Significant weakness such that the person is unable to walk</li>
<li>Inability to feed</li>
<li>Two or more <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convulsions" title="Convulsions">convulsions</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Low_blood_pressure" title="Low blood pressure">Low blood pressure</a> (less than 70 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/MmHg" title="MmHg">mmHg</a> in adults and 50 mmHg in children)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Respiratory_distress" title="Respiratory distress">Breathing problems</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Circulatory_shock" title="Circulatory shock">Circulatory shock</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kidney_failure" title="Kidney failure">Kidney failure</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haemoglobin" title="Haemoglobin">haemoglobin</a> in the urine</li>
<li>Bleeding problems, or hemoglobin less than 50 g/L (5 g/dL)</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pulmonary_oedema" title="Pulmonary oedema">Pulmonary oedema</a></li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Blood_glucose" title="Blood glucose">Blood glucose</a> less than 2.2 mmol/L (40 mg/dL)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Acidosis" title="Acidosis">Acidosis</a> or <a href="/wiki/Lactic_acid" title="Lactic acid">lactate</a> levels of greater than 5 mmol/L</li>
<li>A parasite level in the blood of greater than 100,000 per <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Microlitre" title="Microlitre">microlitre</a> (μL) in low-intensity transmission areas, or 250,000 per μL in high-intensity transmission areas</li></ul>
<p>Cerebral malaria is defined as a severe <i>P. falciparum</i>-malaria presenting with neurological symptoms, including coma (with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Glasgow_coma_scale" title="Glasgow coma scale">Glasgow coma scale</a> less than 11, or a <a href="/wiki/Blantyre_coma_scale" title="Blantyre coma scale">Blantyre coma scale</a> less than 3), or with a coma that lasts longer than 30 minutes after a seizure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prevention">Prevention</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Prevention">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Anopheles_stephensi.jpeg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2008" data-file-width="3032" decoding="async" height="146" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Anopheles_stephensi.jpeg/220px-Anopheles_stephensi.jpeg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Anopheles_stephensi.jpeg/330px-Anopheles_stephensi.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7e/Anopheles_stephensi.jpeg/440px-Anopheles_stephensi.jpeg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Anopheles_stephensi.jpeg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An <i><a href="/wiki/Anopheles_stephensi" title="Anopheles stephensi">Anopheles stephensi</a></i> mosquito shortly after obtaining blood from a human (the droplet of blood is expelled as a surplus). This mosquito is a vector of malaria, and mosquito control is an effective way of reducing its incidence.</div></div></div>
<p>Methods used to prevent malaria include medications, mosquito elimination and the prevention of bites. As of 2020, there is one <a href="/wiki/Malaria_vaccine" title="Malaria vaccine">vaccine for malaria</a> (known as <a href="/wiki/RTS,S" title="RTS,S">RTS,S</a>) which is licensed for use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016_11-2"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016-11">[11]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2020Fact_10-1"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2020Fact-10">[10]</a></sup> The presence of malaria in an area requires a combination of high human population density, high anopheles mosquito population density and high rates of transmission from humans to mosquitoes and from mosquitoes to humans. If any of these is lowered sufficiently, the parasite eventually disappears from that area, as happened in North America, Europe, and parts of the Middle East. However, unless the parasite is eliminated from the whole world, it could re-establish if conditions revert to a combination that favors the parasite's reproduction. Furthermore, the cost per person of eliminating anopheles mosquitoes rises with decreasing population density, making it economically unfeasible in some areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-whqlibdoc_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-whqlibdoc-65">[65]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prevention of malaria may be more cost-effective than treatment of the disease in the long run, but the <a href="/wiki/Capital_cost" title="Capital cost">initial costs</a> required are out of reach of many of the world's poorest people. There is a wide difference in the costs of control (i.e. maintenance of low endemicity) and elimination programs between countries. For example, in China—whose government in 2010 announced a strategy to pursue malaria elimination in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chinese_province" title="Chinese province">Chinese provinces</a>—the required investment is a small proportion of public expenditure on health. In contrast, a similar programme in Tanzania would cost an estimated one-fifth of the public health budget.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sabot_2010_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sabot_2010-66">[66]</a></sup> In 2021, the World Health Organization confirms that China has eliminated malaria. <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-67"><a href="#cite_note-67">[67]</a></sup>
</p><p>In areas where malaria is common, children under five years old often have <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaemia" title="Anaemia">anaemia</a>, which is sometimes due to malaria. Giving children with anaemia in these areas preventive antimalarial medication improves red blood cell levels slightly but does not affect the risk of death or need for hospitalisation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mosquito_control">Mosquito control</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Mosquito control">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Mosquito_control" title="Mosquito control">Mosquito control</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mansprayingkeroseneoil.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="650" data-file-width="507" decoding="async" height="282" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Mansprayingkeroseneoil.jpg/220px-Mansprayingkeroseneoil.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Mansprayingkeroseneoil.jpg/330px-Mansprayingkeroseneoil.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/41/Mansprayingkeroseneoil.jpg/440px-Mansprayingkeroseneoil.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mansprayingkeroseneoil.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Man spraying kerosene oil in standing water, <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal_Zone" title="Panama Canal Zone">Panama Canal Zone</a>, 1912</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Vector_control" title="Vector control">Vector control</a> refers to methods used to decrease malaria by reducing the levels of transmission by mosquitoes. For individual protection, the most effective <a href="/wiki/Insect_repellent" title="Insect repellent">insect repellents</a> are based on <a href="/wiki/DEET" title="DEET">DEET</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Picaridin" title="Picaridin">picaridin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kajfasz_2009_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kajfasz_2009-69">[69]</a></sup> However, there is insufficient evidence that mosquito repellents can prevent malaria infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insecticide-treated_net" title="Insecticide-treated net">Insecticide-treated nets</a> (ITNs) and <a href="/wiki/Indoor_residual_spraying" title="Indoor residual spraying">indoor residual spraying</a> (IRS) are effective, have been commonly used to prevent malaria, and their use has contributed significantly to the decrease in malaria in the 21st century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-:3-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pluess_2010_73-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pluess_2010-73">[73]</a></sup> ITNs and IRS may not be sufficient to eliminate the disease, as these interventions depend on how many people use nets, how many gaps in insecticide there are (low coverage areas), if people are not protected when outside of the home, and an increase in mosquitoes that are resistant to insecticides.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_71-1"><a href="#cite_note-:3-71">[71]</a></sup> Modifications to people's houses to prevent mosquito exposure may be an important long term prevention measure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_71-2"><a href="#cite_note-:3-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mosquitoes-Killedy-By-DDT-Lake-Victoria.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2937" data-file-width="3933" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Mosquitoes-Killedy-By-DDT-Lake-Victoria.JPG/220px-Mosquitoes-Killedy-By-DDT-Lake-Victoria.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Mosquitoes-Killedy-By-DDT-Lake-Victoria.JPG/330px-Mosquitoes-Killedy-By-DDT-Lake-Victoria.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/24/Mosquitoes-Killedy-By-DDT-Lake-Victoria.JPG/440px-Mosquitoes-Killedy-By-DDT-Lake-Victoria.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mosquitoes-Killedy-By-DDT-Lake-Victoria.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Walls where indoor residual spraying of DDT has been applied. The mosquitoes remain on the wall until they fall down dead on the floor.</div></div></div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Insecticide-treated_nets">Insecticide-treated nets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Insecticide-treated nets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mosquitonet149.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="909" data-file-width="739" decoding="async" height="271" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Mosquitonet149.jpg/220px-Mosquitonet149.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Mosquitonet149.jpg/330px-Mosquitonet149.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/5d/Mosquitonet149.jpg/440px-Mosquitonet149.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mosquitonet149.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A mosquito net in use.</div></div></div>
<p>Mosquito nets help keep mosquitoes away from people and reduce infection rates and transmission of malaria. Nets are not a perfect barrier and are often treated with an insecticide designed to kill the mosquito before it has time to find a way past the net. Insecticide-treated nets (ITNs) are estimated to be twice as effective as untreated nets and offer greater than 70% protection compared with no net.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raghavendra_2011_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-Raghavendra_2011-74">[74]</a></sup> Between 2000 and 2008, the use of ITNs saved the lives of an estimated 250,000 infants in Sub-Saharan Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howitt_2012_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-Howitt_2012-75">[75]</a></sup> About 13% of households in Sub-Saharan countries owned ITNs in 2007<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Miller_2007_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-Miller_2007-76">[76]</a></sup> and 31% of African households were estimated to own at least one ITN in 2008. In 2000, 1.7 million (1.8%) African children living in areas of the world where malaria is common were protected by an ITN. That number increased to 20.3 million (18.5%) African children using ITNs in 2007, leaving 89.6 million children unprotected<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Noor_2009_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-Noor_2009-77">[77]</a></sup> and to 68% African children using mosquito nets in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNICEF2015_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-UNICEF2015-78">[78]</a></sup> Most nets are impregnated with <a href="/wiki/Pyrethroid" title="Pyrethroid">pyrethroids</a>, a class of insecticides with low <a href="/wiki/Toxicity" title="Toxicity">toxicity</a>. They are most effective when used from dusk to dawn.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> It is recommended to hang a large "bed net" above the center of a bed and either tuck the edges under the mattress or make sure it is large enough such that it touches the ground.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> ITNs are beneficial towards pregnancy outcomes in malaria-endemic regions in Africa but more data is needed in Asia and Latin America.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup>
</p><p>In areas of high malaria resistance, piperonyl butoxide (PBO) combined with pyrethroids in mosquito netting is effective in reducing malaria infection rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-:4-82">[82]</a></sup>  Questions remain concerning the durability of PBO on nets as the impact on mosquito mortality was not sustained after twenty washes in experimental trials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:4_82-1"><a href="#cite_note-:4-82">[82]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Indoor_residual_spraying">Indoor residual spraying</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Indoor residual spraying">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Indoor residual spraying is the spraying of insecticides on the walls inside a home. After feeding, many mosquitoes rest on a nearby surface while digesting the bloodmeal, so if the walls of houses have been coated with insecticides, the resting mosquitoes can be killed before they can bite another person and transfer the malaria parasite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enayati_2010_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-Enayati_2010-83">[83]</a></sup> As of 2006, the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> recommends 12 insecticides in IRS operations, including <a href="/wiki/DDT#Use_against_malaria" title="DDT">DDT</a> and the pyrethroids <a href="/wiki/Cyfluthrin" title="Cyfluthrin">cyfluthrin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Deltamethrin" title="Deltamethrin">deltamethrin</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO_Indoor_Residual_Spraying_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-WHO_Indoor_Residual_Spraying-84">[84]</a></sup> This public health use of small amounts of DDT is permitted under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stockholm_Convention" title="Stockholm Convention">Stockholm Convention</a>, which prohibits its agricultural use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_den_Berg_2009_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-van_den_Berg_2009-85">[85]</a></sup> One problem with all forms of IRS is <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Insecticide_resistance" title="Insecticide resistance">insecticide resistance</a>. Mosquitoes affected by IRS tend to rest and live indoors, and due to the irritation caused by spraying, their descendants tend to rest and live outdoors, meaning that they are less affected by the IRS.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pates_2005_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pates_2005-86">[86]</a></sup> Communities using insecticide treated nets, in addition to indoor residual spraying with 'non-pyrethroid-like' insecticides found associated reductions in malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-:7-87">[87]</a></sup>  Additionally, the use of 'pyrethroid-like' insecticides in addition to indoor residual spraying did not result in a detectable additional benefit in communities using insecticide treated nets.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:7_87-1"><a href="#cite_note-:7-87">[87]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Housing_modifications">Housing modifications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Housing modifications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Housing is a risk factor for malaria and modifying the house as a prevention measure may be a sustainable strategy that does not rely on the effectiveness of insecticides such as <a href="/wiki/Pyrethroid" title="Pyrethroid">pyrethroids</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_71-3"><a href="#cite_note-:3-71">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> The physical environment inside and outside the home that may improve the density of mosquitoes are considerations. Examples of potential modifications include how close the home is to mosquito breeding sites, drainage and water supply near the home, availability of mosquito resting sites (vegetation around the home), the proximity to live stock and domestic animals, and physical improvements or modifications to the design of the home to prevent mosquitoes from entering.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:3_71-4"><a href="#cite_note-:3-71">[71]</a></sup>
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_mosquito_control_methods">Other mosquito control methods</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Other mosquito control methods">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>People have tried a number of other methods to reduce mosquito bites and slow the spread of malaria. Efforts to decrease mosquito larvae by decreasing the availability of open water where they develop, or by adding substances to decrease their development, are effective in some locations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tusting_2013_89-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tusting_2013-89">[89]</a></sup> Electronic mosquito repellent devices, which make very high-frequency sounds that are supposed to keep female mosquitoes away, have no supporting evidence of effectiveness.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Enayati_2007_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-Enayati_2007-90">[90]</a></sup> There is a low certainty evidence that <a href="/wiki/Fogging_(insect_control)" title="Fogging (insect control)">fogging</a> may have an effect on malaria transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Larviciding by hand delivery of chemical or microbial insecticides into water bodies containing low larval distribution may reduce malarial transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> There is insufficient evidence to determine whether larvivorous fish can decrease mosquito density and transmission in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medications">Medications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Medications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Malaria_prophylaxis" title="Malaria prophylaxis">Malaria prophylaxis</a></div>
<p>There are a number of medications that can help prevent or interrupt malaria in travellers to places where infection is common. Many of these medications are also used in treatment. In places where <i>Plasmodium</i> is resistant to one or more medications, three medications—<a href="/wiki/Mefloquine" title="Mefloquine">mefloquine</a>, <a href="/wiki/Doxycycline" title="Doxycycline">doxycycline</a>, or the combination of <a href="/wiki/Atovaquone/proguanil" title="Atovaquone/proguanil">atovaquone/proguanil</a> (<i>Malarone</i>)—are frequently used for prevention.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tic2017_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tic2017-94">[94]</a></sup> Doxycycline and the atovaquone/proguanil are better tolerated while mefloquine is taken once a week.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tic2017_94-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tic2017-94">[94]</a></sup> Areas of the world with <a href="/wiki/Chloroquine" title="Chloroquine">chloroquine</a>-sensitive malaria are uncommon.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Antimalarial mass drug administration to an entire population at the same time may reduce the risk of contracting malaria in the population, however the effectiveness of mass drug administration may vary depending on the prevalence of malaria in the area.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_96-0"><a href="#cite_note-:6-96">[96]</a></sup> Other factors such as drug administration plus other protective measures such as mosiquito control, the proportion of people treated in the area, and the risk of reinfection with malaria may play a role in the effectiveness of mass drug treatment approaches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:6_96-1"><a href="#cite_note-:6-96">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>The protective effect does not begin immediately, and people visiting areas where malaria exists usually start taking the drugs one to two weeks before they arrive, and continue taking them for four weeks after leaving (except for atovaquone/proguanil, which only needs to be started two days before and continued for seven days afterward).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Freedman_2008_97-0"><a href="#cite_note-Freedman_2008-97">[97]</a></sup> The use of preventive drugs is often not practical for those who live in areas where malaria exists, and their use is usually given only to pregnant women and short-term visitors. This is due to the cost of the drugs, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adverse_effect_(medicine)" title="Adverse effect (medicine)">side effects</a> from long-term use, and the difficulty in obtaining antimalarial drugs outside of wealthy nations.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fernando_2011_98-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fernando_2011-98">[98]</a></sup> During pregnancy, medication to prevent malaria has been found to improve the weight of the baby at birth and decrease the risk of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Anaemia" title="Anaemia">anaemia</a> in the mother.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> The use of preventive drugs where malaria-bearing mosquitoes are present may encourage the development of partial resistance.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Turschner_2009_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-Turschner_2009-100">[100]</a></sup>
</p><p>Giving antimalarial drugs to infants through intermittent preventive therapy can reduce the risk of having malaria infection, hospital admission, and anaemia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup>
</p><p>Mefloquine is more effective than sulfadoxine-pyrimethamine in preventing malaria for HIV-negative pregnant women. Cotrimoxazole is effective in preventing malaria infection and reduce the risk of getting anaemia in HIV-positive women.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Giving sulfadoxine-pyrimethamine for three or more doses as intermittent preventive therapy is superior than two doses for HIV-positive women living in malaria-endemic areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>
</p><p>Prompt treatment of confirmed cases with artemisinin-based combination therapies (ACTs) may also reduce transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Others">Others</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Others">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
