<p>Community participation and <a href="/wiki/Health_education" title="Health education">health education</a> strategies promoting awareness of malaria and the importance of control measures have been successfully used to reduce the incidence of malaria in some areas of the developing world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lalloo_2006_105-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lalloo_2006-105">[105]</a></sup> Recognising the disease in the early stages can prevent it from becoming fatal. Education can also inform people to cover over areas of stagnant, still water, such as water tanks that are ideal breeding grounds for the parasite and mosquito, thus cutting down the risk of the transmission between people. This is generally used in urban areas where there are large centers of population in a confined space and transmission would be most likely in these areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mehlhorn_2008_106-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mehlhorn_2008-106">[106]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Intermittent_preventive_therapy" title="Intermittent preventive therapy">Intermittent preventive therapy</a> is another intervention that has been used successfully to control malaria in pregnant women and infants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bardají_2012_107-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bardají_2012-107">[107]</a></sup> and in preschool children where transmission is seasonal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meremikwu_2012_108-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meremikwu_2012-108">[108]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Treatment">Treatment</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Treatment">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%22British_India%22,_six_stages_of_malaria._Wellcome_L0022443.jpg"><img alt='Advertisement entitled "The Mosquito Danger". Includes 6 panel cartoon:#1 breadwinner has malaria, family starving; #2 wife selling ornaments; #3 doctor administers quinine; #4 patient recovers; #5 doctor indicating that quinine can be obtained from post office if needed again; #6 man who refused quinine, dead on stretcher.' class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1648" data-file-width="1142" decoding="async" height="317" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/%22British_India%22%2C_six_stages_of_malaria._Wellcome_L0022443.jpg/220px-%22British_India%22%2C_six_stages_of_malaria._Wellcome_L0022443.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/%22British_India%22%2C_six_stages_of_malaria._Wellcome_L0022443.jpg/330px-%22British_India%22%2C_six_stages_of_malaria._Wellcome_L0022443.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a0/%22British_India%22%2C_six_stages_of_malaria._Wellcome_L0022443.jpg/440px-%22British_India%22%2C_six_stages_of_malaria._Wellcome_L0022443.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%22British_India%22,_six_stages_of_malaria._Wellcome_L0022443.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>An advertisement for <a href="/wiki/Quinine" title="Quinine">quinine</a> as a malaria treatment from 1927.</div></div></div>
<p>Malaria is treated with <a href="/wiki/Antimalarial_medication" title="Antimalarial medication">antimalarial medications</a>; the ones used depends on the type and severity of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> While <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antipyretics" title="Antipyretics">medications against fever</a> are commonly used, their effects on outcomes are not clear.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meremikwu_2012b_111-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meremikwu_2012b-111">[111]</a></sup> Providing free antimalarial drugs to households may reduce childhood deaths when used appropriately. Programmes which presumptively treat all causes of fever with antimalarial drugs may lead to overuse of antimalarials and undertreat other causes of fever. Nevertheless, the use of malaria rapid-diagnostic kits can help to reduce over-usage of antimalarials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-113"><a href="#cite_note-113">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Uncomplicated_malaria">Uncomplicated malaria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Uncomplicated malaria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Simple or uncomplicated malaria may be treated with oral medications. Artemisinin drugs are effective and safe in treating uncomplicated malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-114"><a href="#cite_note-114">[114]</a></sup> Artemisinin in combination with other antimalarials (known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Artemisinin-combination_therapy" title="Artemisinin-combination therapy">artemisinin-combination therapy</a>, or ACT) is about 90% effective when used to treat uncomplicated malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howitt_2012_75-1"><a href="#cite_note-Howitt_2012-75">[75]</a></sup> The most effective treatment for <i>P. falciparum</i> infection is the use of ACT, which decreases resistance to any single drug component.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-115"><a href="#cite_note-115">[115]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kokwaro_2009_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kokwaro_2009-116">[116]</a></sup> Artemether-lumefantrine (six-dose regimen) is more effective than the artemether-lumefantrine (four-dose regimen) or other regimens not containing artemisinin derivatives in treating falciparum malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-117"><a href="#cite_note-117">[117]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-118"><a href="#cite_note-118">[118]</a></sup> Another recommended combination is <a href="/wiki/Dihydroartemisinin" title="Dihydroartemisinin">dihydroartemisinin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Piperaquine" title="Piperaquine">piperaquine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-119"><a href="#cite_note-119">[119]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Keating_2012_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-Keating_2012-120">[120]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-121"><a href="#cite_note-121">[121]</a></sup> Artemisinin-naphthoquine combination therapy showed promising results in treating falciparum malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-122"><a href="#cite_note-122">[122]</a></sup> However, more research is needed to establish its efficacy as a reliable treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-123"><a href="#cite_note-123">[123]</a></sup> Artesunate plus mefloquine performs better than mefloquine alone in treating uncomplicated falciparum malaria in low transmission settings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-124"><a href="#cite_note-124">[124]</a></sup> Atovaquone-proguanil is effective against uncomplicated falciparum with a possible failure rate of 5% to 10%; the addition of artesunate may reduce failure rate.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-125"><a href="#cite_note-125">[125]</a></sup> Azithromycin monotherapy or combination therapy has not shown effectiveness in treating plasmodium or vivax malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-126"><a href="#cite_note-126">[126]</a></sup> Amodiaquine plus sulfadoxine-pyrimethamine may achieve less treatment failures when compared to sulfadoxine-pyrimethamine alone in uncomplicated falciparum malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-127"><a href="#cite_note-127">[127]</a></sup> There is insufficient data on chlorproguanil-dapsone in treating uncomplicated falciparum malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-128"><a href="#cite_note-128">[128]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-129"><a href="#cite_note-129">[129]</a></sup> The addition of primaquine with artemisinin-based combination therapy for falciparum malaria reduces its transmission at day 3-4 and day 8 of infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-130"><a href="#cite_note-130">[130]</a></sup> Sulfadoxine-pyrimethamine plus artesunate is better than sulfadoxine-pyrimethamine plus amodiaquine in controlling treatment failure at day 28. However, the latter is better than the former in reducing gametocytes in blood at day 7.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-131"><a href="#cite_note-131">[131]</a></sup>
</p><p>Infection with <i>P. vivax</i>, <i>P. ovale</i> or <i>P. malariae</i> usually does not require hospitalisation. Treatment of <i>P. vivax</i> requires both treatment of blood stages (with chloroquine or artemisinin-based combination therapy) and clearance of liver forms with an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/8-aminoquinoline" title="8-aminoquinoline">8-aminoquinoline</a> agent such as <a href="/wiki/Primaquine" title="Primaquine">primaquine</a> or <a href="/wiki/Tafenoquine" title="Tafenoquine">tafenoquine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Waters_2012_132-0"><a href="#cite_note-Waters_2012-132">[132]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-133"><a href="#cite_note-133">[133]</a></sup>
</p><p>To treat malaria during pregnancy, the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">WHO</a> recommends the use of quinine plus <a href="/wiki/Clindamycin" title="Clindamycin">clindamycin</a> early in the pregnancy (1st trimester), and ACT in later stages (2nd and 3rd trimesters).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-134"><a href="#cite_note-134">[134]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Manyando_2012_135-0"><a href="#cite_note-Manyando_2012-135">[135]</a></sup> There is limited safety data on the antimalarial drugs in pregnancy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-136"><a href="#cite_note-136">[136]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Severe_and_complicated_malaria">Severe and complicated malaria</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Severe and complicated malaria">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Cases of severe and complicated malaria are almost always caused by infection with <i>P. falciparum</i>. The other species usually cause only febrile disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-137"><a href="#cite_note-137">[137]</a></sup> Severe and complicated malaria cases are medical emergencies since mortality rates are high (10% to 50%).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-138"><a href="#cite_note-138">[138]</a></sup>
</p><p>Recommended treatment for severe malaria is the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parenteral_administration" title="Parenteral administration">intravenous</a> use of antimalarial drugs. For severe malaria, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parenteral" title="Parenteral">parenteral</a> artesunate was superior to quinine in both children and adults.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-139"><a href="#cite_note-139">[139]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sinclair_2012_140-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sinclair_2012-140">[140]</a></sup> In another systematic review, artemisinin derivatives (artemether and arteether) were as efficacious as quinine in the treatment of cerebral malaria in children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-141"><a href="#cite_note-141">[141]</a></sup> Treatment of severe malaria involves supportive measures that are best done in a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Critical_care_unit" title="Critical care unit">critical care unit</a>. This includes the management of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hyperpyrexia" title="Hyperpyrexia">high fevers</a> and the seizures that may result from it. It also includes monitoring for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Respiratory_depression" title="Respiratory depression">poor breathing effort</a>, low blood sugar, and <a href="/wiki/Hypokalemia" title="Hypokalemia">low blood potassium</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sarkar_2009_33-1"><a href="#cite_note-Sarkar_2009-33">[33]</a></sup> Artemisinin derivatives have the same or better efficacy than quinolones in preventing deaths in severe or complicated malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-142"><a href="#cite_note-142">[142]</a></sup> Quinine <a href="/wiki/Loading_dose" title="Loading dose">loading dose</a> helps to shorten the duration of fever and increases parasite clearance from the body.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-143"><a href="#cite_note-143">[143]</a></sup> There is no difference in effectiveness when using intrarectal quinine compared to intravenous or intramuscular quinine in treating uncomplicated/complicated falciparum malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-144"><a href="#cite_note-144">[144]</a></sup> There is insufficient evidence for intramuscular arteether to treat severe malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-145"><a href="#cite_note-145">[145]</a></sup> The provision of rectal artesunate before transfer to hospital may reduce the rate of death for children with severe malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-146"><a href="#cite_note-146">[146]</a></sup>
</p><p>Cerebral malaria is the form of severe and complicated malaria with the worst neurological symptoms.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-147"><a href="#cite_note-147">[147]</a></sup> There is insufficient data on whether osmotic agents such as mannitol or urea are effective in treating cerebral malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-148"><a href="#cite_note-148">[148]</a></sup> Routine phenobarbitone in cerebral malaria is associated with fewer <a href="/wiki/Convulsion" title="Convulsion">convulsions</a> but possibly more deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-149"><a href="#cite_note-149">[149]</a></sup> There is no evidence that steroids would bring treatment benefits for cerebral malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-150"><a href="#cite_note-150">[150]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Managing Cerebral Malaria</b>
</p><p>Cerebral malaria usually makes a patient comatose, if the cause of the coma is in doubt, test for other locally prevalent causes of encephalopathy (bacterial, viral or fungal infection) should be carried out. In areas where there is a high prevalence of malaria infection (e.g. tropical region) treatment can start without testing first.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC2_31-1"><a href="#cite_note-CDC2-31">[31]</a></sup> To manage the cerebral malaria when confirmed the following can be done:
</p>
<ul><li>Patients in coma should be given meticulous nursing care ( monitor vital signs, turn patient every 2 hours, avoid lying the patient in a wet bed etc.)</li>
<li>A sterile urethral catheter should be inserted to help with urinating</li>
<li>To aspirate stomach content, a sterile nasogastric tube should be inserted.</li>
<li>In the occasion of convulsions, a slow intravenous injection of benzodiazepine is administered.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-151"><a href="#cite_note-151">[151]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>There is insufficient evidence to show that blood transfusion is useful in either reducing deaths for children with severe anaemia or in improving their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Haematocrit" title="Haematocrit">haematocrit</a> in one month.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-152"><a href="#cite_note-152">[152]</a></sup> There is insufficient evidence that iron chelating agents such as deferoxamine and deferiprone improve outcomes of those with malaria falciparum infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-153"><a href="#cite_note-153">[153]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Resistance">Resistance</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Resistance">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Drug_resistance" title="Drug resistance">Drug resistance</a> poses a growing problem in 21st-century malaria treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SinhaMedhi2014_154-0"><a href="#cite_note-SinhaMedhi2014-154">[154]</a></sup> In the 2000s (decade), malaria with partial resistance to artemisins emerged in Southeast Asia.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-O'Brien_2011_155-0"><a href="#cite_note-O'Brien_2011-155">[155]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fairhurst_2012_156-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fairhurst_2012-156">[156]</a></sup> Resistance is now common against all classes of antimalarial drugs apart from <a href="/wiki/Artemisinin" title="Artemisinin">artemisinins</a>. Treatment of resistant strains became increasingly dependent on this class of drugs. The cost of artemisinins limits their use in the developing world.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-White_2008_157-0"><a href="#cite_note-White_2008-157">[157]</a></sup> Malaria strains found on the Cambodia–Thailand border are resistant to combination therapies that include artemisinins, and may, therefore, be untreatable.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Wongsrichanalai_2008_158-0"><a href="#cite_note-Wongsrichanalai_2008-158">[158]</a></sup> Exposure of the parasite population to artemisinin monotherapies in subtherapeutic doses for over 30 years and the availability of substandard artemisinins likely drove the selection of the resistant phenotype.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dondorp_2010_159-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dondorp_2010-159">[159]</a></sup> Resistance to artemisinin has been detected in Cambodia, Myanmar, Thailand, and Vietnam,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-160"><a href="#cite_note-160">[160]</a></sup> and there has been emerging resistance in Laos.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Briggs_161-0"><a href="#cite_note-Briggs-161">[161]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ashley_2014_162-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ashley_2014-162">[162]</a></sup> Resistance to the combination of artemisinin and piperaquine was first detected in 2013 in Cambodia, and by 2019 had spread across Cambodia and into <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a> and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a> (with up to 80 percent of malaria parasites resistant in some regions).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-163"><a href="#cite_note-163">[163]</a></sup>
</p><p>There is insufficient evidence in unit packaged antimalarial drugs in preventing treatment failures of malaria infection. However, if supported by training of healthcare providers and patient information, there is improvement in compliance of those receiving treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-164"><a href="#cite_note-164">[164]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Prognosis">Prognosis</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Prognosis">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malaria_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Malaria_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/290px-Malaria_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Malaria_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/435px-Malaria_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d8/Malaria_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg/580px-Malaria_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Malaria_world_map_-_DALY_-_WHO2004.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Disability-adjusted_life_year" title="Disability-adjusted life year">Disability-adjusted life year</a> for malaria per 100,000 inhabitants in 2004<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r998391716">.mw-parser-output .div-col{margin-top:0.3em;column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .div-col-small{font-size:90%}.mw-parser-output .div-col-rules{column-rule:1px solid #aaa}.mw-parser-output .div-col dl,.mw-parser-output .div-col ol,.mw-parser-output .div-col ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .div-col li,.mw-parser-output .div-col dd{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="div-col" style="column-width: 10em;"> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#b3b3b3; color:black;"> </span>    no data</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffff65; color:black;"> </span>    &lt;10</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#fff200; color:black;"> </span>    0–100</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffdc00; color:black;"> </span>  100–500</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffc600; color:black;"> </span>  500–1000</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffb000; color:black;"> </span> 1000–1500</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff9a00; color:black;"> </span> 1500–2000</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff8400; color:black;"> </span> 2000–2500</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff6e00; color:black;"> </span> 2500–2750</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff5800; color:black;"> </span> 2750–3000</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff4200; color:black;"> </span> 3000–3250</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff2c00; color:black;"> </span> 3250–3500</div> <link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#cb0000; color:white;"> </span>    ≥3500</div> </div></div></div></div>
<p>When properly treated, people with malaria can usually expect a complete recovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Malaria_165-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Malaria-165">[165]</a></sup> However, severe malaria can progress extremely rapidly and cause death within hours or days.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Trampuz_2003_166-0"><a href="#cite_note-Trampuz_2003-166">[166]</a></sup> In the most severe cases of the disease, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatality_rate" title="Fatality rate">fatality rates</a> can reach 20%, even with intensive care and treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nadjm_2012_9-5"><a href="#cite_note-Nadjm_2012-9">[9]</a></sup> Over the longer term, developmental impairments have been documented in children who have had episodes of severe malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fernando_2010_167-0"><a href="#cite_note-Fernando_2010-167">[167]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chronic_(medicine)" title="Chronic (medicine)">Chronic</a> infection without severe disease can occur in an immune-deficiency syndrome associated with a decreased responsiveness to <i><a href="/wiki/Salmonella" title="Salmonella">Salmonella</a></i> bacteria and the <a href="/wiki/Epstein%E2%80%93Barr_virus" title="Epstein–Barr virus">Epstein–Barr virus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Riley_2013_168-0"><a href="#cite_note-Riley_2013-168">[168]</a></sup>
</p><p>During childhood, malaria causes anaemia during a period of rapid brain development, and also direct brain damage resulting from cerebral malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fernando_2010_167-1"><a href="#cite_note-Fernando_2010-167">[167]</a></sup> Some survivors of cerebral malaria have an increased risk of neurological and cognitive deficits, <a href="/wiki/Emotional_and_behavioral_disorders" title="Emotional and behavioral disorders">behavioural disorders</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Idro_2010_169-0"><a href="#cite_note-Idro_2010-169">[169]</a></sup> Malaria prophylaxis was shown to improve cognitive function and school performance in <a href="/wiki/Clinical_trial" title="Clinical trial">clinical trials</a> when compared to <a href="/wiki/Placebo" title="Placebo">placebo</a> groups.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fernando_2010_167-2"><a href="#cite_note-Fernando_2010-167">[167]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Epidemiology">Epidemiology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Epidemiology">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malaria_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="415" data-file-width="940" decoding="async" height="128" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Malaria_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg/290px-Malaria_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Malaria_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg/435px-Malaria_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/96/Malaria_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg/580px-Malaria_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Malaria_world_map-Deaths_per_million_persons-WHO2012.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Deaths due to malaria per million persons in 2012 <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1054258005">.mw-parser-output .refbegin{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul{margin-left:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{margin-left:0;padding-left:3.2em;text-indent:-3.2em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul,.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents ul li{list-style:none}@media(max-width:720px){.mw-parser-output .refbegin-hanging-indents>ul>li{padding-left:1.6em;text-indent:-1.6em}}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns ul{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .refbegin-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}</style><div class="refbegin refbegin-columns references-column-count references-column-count-3" style="column-count: 3;"><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffff20; color:black;"> </span> 0–0</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ffa020; color:black;"> </span> 1–2</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff9a20; color:black;"> </span> 3–54</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#f08015; color:black;"> </span> 55–325</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#e06815; color:black;"> </span> 326–679</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d85010; color:black;"> </span> 680–949</div><link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#d02010; color:white;"> </span> 950–1,358</div></div></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:292px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World-map-of-past-and-current-malaria-prevalence-world-development-report-2009.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="617" data-file-width="1240" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/World-map-of-past-and-current-malaria-prevalence-world-development-report-2009.png/290px-World-map-of-past-and-current-malaria-prevalence-world-development-report-2009.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/World-map-of-past-and-current-malaria-prevalence-world-development-report-2009.png/435px-World-map-of-past-and-current-malaria-prevalence-world-development-report-2009.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/ae/World-map-of-past-and-current-malaria-prevalence-world-development-report-2009.png/580px-World-map-of-past-and-current-malaria-prevalence-world-development-report-2009.png 2x" width="290"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World-map-of-past-and-current-malaria-prevalence-world-development-report-2009.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Past and current malaria prevalence in 2009</div></div></div>
<p>The WHO estimates that in 2019 there were 229 million new cases of malaria resulting in 409,000 deaths.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2020_4-3"><a href="#cite_note-who2020-4">[4]</a></sup> Children under 5 years old are the most affected, accounting for 67% of malaria deaths worldwide in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-who2020_4-4"><a href="#cite_note-who2020-4">[4]</a></sup> About 125 million pregnant women are at risk of infection each year; in <a href="/wiki/Sub-Saharan_Africa" title="Sub-Saharan Africa">Sub-Saharan Africa</a>, maternal malaria is associated with up to 200,000 estimated infant deaths yearly.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hartman_2010_29-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hartman_2010-29">[29]</a></sup> There are about 10,000 malaria cases per year in Western Europe, and 1300–1500 in the United States.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Taylor_2012_25-1"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor_2012-25">[25]</a></sup> The United States eradicated malaria as a major public health concern in 1951,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-170"><a href="#cite_note-170">[170]</a></sup> though small outbreaks persist.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-171"><a href="#cite_note-171">[171]</a></sup> About 900 people died from the disease in Europe between 1993 and 2003.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kajfasz_2009_69-1"><a href="#cite_note-Kajfasz_2009-69">[69]</a></sup> Both the global incidence of disease and resulting mortality have declined in recent years. According to the WHO and UNICEF, deaths attributable to malaria in 2015 were reduced by 60%<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-UNICEF2015_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-UNICEF2015-78">[78]</a></sup> from a 2000 estimate of 985,000, largely due to the widespread use of insecticide-treated nets and artemisinin-based combination therapies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Howitt_2012_75-2"><a href="#cite_note-Howitt_2012-75">[75]</a></sup> In 2012, there were 207 million cases of malaria. That year, the disease is estimated to have killed between 473,000 and 789,000 people, many of whom were children in Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-16"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> Efforts at decreasing the disease in Africa since 2000 have been partially effective, with rates of the disease dropping by an estimated forty percent on the continent.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-172"><a href="#cite_note-172">[172]</a></sup>
</p><p>Malaria is presently endemic in a broad band around the equator, in areas of the Americas, many parts of Asia, and much of Africa; in Sub-Saharan Africa, 85–90% of malaria fatalities occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Layne_2006_173-0"><a href="#cite_note-Layne_2006-173">[173]</a></sup> An estimate for 2009 reported that countries with the highest death rate per 100,000 of population were <a href="/wiki/Ivory_Coast" title="Ivory Coast">Ivory Coast</a> (86.15), <a href="/wiki/Angola" title="Angola">Angola</a> (56.93) and <a href="/wiki/Burkina_Faso" title="Burkina Faso">Burkina Faso</a> (50.66).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Provost_2011_174-0"><a href="#cite_note-Provost_2011-174">[174]</a></sup> A 2010 estimate indicated the deadliest countries per population were Burkina Faso, <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mali" title="Mali">Mali</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-lancet-glob-mal-mort_175-0"><a href="#cite_note-lancet-glob-mal-mort-175">[175]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Malaria_Atlas_Project" title="Malaria Atlas Project">Malaria Atlas Project</a> aims to map global <a href="/wiki/Endemic_(epidemiology)" title="Endemic (epidemiology)">levels of malaria</a>, providing a way to determine the global spatial limits of the disease and to assess <a href="/wiki/Disease_burden" title="Disease burden">disease burden</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guerra_2007_176-0"><a href="#cite_note-Guerra_2007-176">[176]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hay_2010_177-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hay_2010-177">[177]</a></sup> This effort led to the publication of a map of <i>P. falciparum</i> endemicity in 2010 and an update in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gething_2011_178-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gething_2011-178">[178]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-179"><a href="#cite_note-179">[179]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-180"><a href="#cite_note-180">[180]</a></sup> As of 2010, about 100 countries have endemic malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-World_Malaria_Report_2012_181-0"><a href="#cite_note-World_Malaria_Report_2012-181">[181]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Feachem_2010_182-0"><a href="#cite_note-Feachem_2010-182">[182]</a></sup> Every year, 125 million international travellers visit these countries, and more than 30,000 contract the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kajfasz_2009_69-2"><a href="#cite_note-Kajfasz_2009-69">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>The geographic distribution of malaria within large regions is complex, and malaria-afflicted and malaria-free areas are often found close to each other.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_2002_183-0"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood_2002-183">[183]</a></sup> Malaria is prevalent in tropical and subtropical regions because of rainfall, consistent high temperatures and high humidity, along with stagnant waters where mosquito larvae readily mature, providing them with the environment they need for continuous breeding.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Jamieson_2006_184-0"><a href="#cite_note-Jamieson_2006-184">[184]</a></sup> In drier areas, outbreaks of malaria have been predicted with reasonable accuracy by mapping rainfall.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Abeku_2007_185-0"><a href="#cite_note-Abeku_2007-185">[185]</a></sup> Malaria is more common in rural areas than in cities. For example, several cities in the <a href="/wiki/Greater_Mekong_Subregion" title="Greater Mekong Subregion">Greater Mekong Subregion</a> of Southeast Asia are essentially malaria-free, but the disease is prevalent in many rural regions, including along international borders and forest fringes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cui_2012_186-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cui_2012-186">[186]</a></sup> In contrast, malaria in Africa is present in both rural and urban areas, though the risk is lower in the larger cities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Machault_2011_187-0"><a href="#cite_note-Machault_2011-187">[187]</a></sup>
</p><p>Since 1900 there has been substantial change in temperature and rainfall over Africa.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-188"><a href="#cite_note-188">[188]</a></sup> However, factors that contribute to how rainfall results in water for mosquito breeding are complex, incorporating the extent to which it is absorbed into soil and vegetation for example, or rates of runoff and evaporation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_189-0"><a href="#cite_note-:2-189">[189]</a></sup> Recent research has provided a more in-depth picture of conditions across Africa, combining a malaria climatic suitability model with a continental-scale model representing real-world hydrological processes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:2_189-1"><a href="#cite_note-:2-189">[189]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="History">History</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: History">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/History_of_malaria" title="History of malaria">History of malaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mosquito-malaria_theory" title="Mosquito-malaria theory">Mosquito-malaria theory</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malaria_pathogens.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1273" data-file-width="986" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Malaria_pathogens.jpg/170px-Malaria_pathogens.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Malaria_pathogens.jpg/255px-Malaria_pathogens.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Malaria_pathogens.jpg/340px-Malaria_pathogens.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Malaria_pathogens.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Ancient malaria oocysts preserved in <a href="/wiki/Dominican_amber" title="Dominican amber">Dominican amber</a></div></div></div>
<p>Although the parasite responsible for <i>P. falciparum</i> malaria has been in existence for 50,000–100,000 years, the population size of the parasite did not increase until about 10,000 years ago, concurrently with advances in agriculture<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Harper_2011_190-0"><a href="#cite_note-Harper_2011-190">[190]</a></sup> and the development of human settlements. Close relatives of the human malaria parasites remain common in chimpanzees. Some evidence suggests that the <i>P. falciparum</i> malaria may have originated in gorillas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Prugnolle_2012_191-0"><a href="#cite_note-Prugnolle_2012-191">[191]</a></sup>
</p><p>References to the unique periodic fevers of malaria are found throughout history.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cox_2002_192-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cox_2002-192">[192]</a></sup> Hippocrates described periodic fevers, labelling them tertian, quartan, subtertian and quotidian.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Blakiston_company_193-0"><a href="#cite_note-The_Blakiston_company-193">[193]</a></sup> The Roman <a href="/wiki/Columella" title="Columella">Columella</a> associated the disease with insects from swamps.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-The_Blakiston_company_193-1"><a href="#cite_note-The_Blakiston_company-193">[193]</a></sup> Malaria may have contributed to the decline of the <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sallares_2001_194-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sallares_2001-194">[194]</a></sup> and was so pervasive in Rome that it was known as the "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Roman_Fever_(disease)" title="Roman Fever (disease)">Roman fever</a>".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sallares_2003_195-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sallares_2003-195">[195]</a></sup> Several regions in ancient Rome were considered at-risk for the disease because of the favourable conditions present for malaria vectors. This included areas such as southern Italy, the island of <a href="/wiki/Sardinia" title="Sardinia">Sardinia</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Pontine_Marshes" title="Pontine Marshes">Pontine Marshes</a>, the lower regions of coastal <a href="/wiki/Etruria" title="Etruria">Etruria</a> and the city of <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> along the <a href="/wiki/Tiber" title="Tiber">Tiber</a>. The presence of stagnant water in these places was preferred by mosquitoes for breeding grounds. Irrigated gardens, swamp-like grounds, run-off from agriculture, and drainage problems from road construction led to the increase of standing water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hays_2005_196-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hays_2005-196">[196]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Ronald_Ross.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1189" data-file-width="837" decoding="async" height="313" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Ronald_Ross.jpg/220px-Ronald_Ross.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Ronald_Ross.jpg/330px-Ronald_Ross.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/Ronald_Ross.jpg/440px-Ronald_Ross.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Ronald_Ross.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>British doctor <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Ross" title="Ronald Ross">Ronald Ross</a> received the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_for_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize for Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize for Physiology or Medicine</a> in 1902 for his work on malaria.</div></div></div>
<p>The term malaria originates from <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Mediaeval</a> <a href="/wiki/Italian_language" title="Italian language">Italian</a>: <i lang="it">mala aria</i>—"<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Miasma_theory_of_disease" title="Miasma theory of disease">bad air</a>"; the disease was formerly called <i>ague</i> or <i>marsh fever</i> due to its association with swamps and marshland.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-197"><a href="#cite_note-197">[197]</a></sup> The term appeared in English at least as early as 1768.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-198"><a href="#cite_note-198">[198]</a></sup> Malaria was once common in most of Europe and North America,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lindemann_1999_199-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lindemann_1999-199">[199]</a></sup> where it is no longer endemic,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gratz_2006_200-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gratz_2006-200">[200]</a></sup> though imported cases do occur.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Webb_2009_201-0"><a href="#cite_note-Webb_2009-201">[201]</a></sup>
</p><p>Malaria is not referenced in the medical books of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mayans" title="Mayans">Mayans</a> or <a href="/wiki/Aztecs" title="Aztecs">Aztecs</a>. European settlers and the West Africans they <a href="/wiki/Atlantic_slave_trade" title="Atlantic slave trade">enslaved</a> likely brought malaria to the Americas starting in the 16th century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-202"><a href="#cite_note-202">[202]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-203"><a href="#cite_note-203">[203]</a></sup>
</p><p>Scientific studies on malaria made their first significant advance in 1880, when <a href="/wiki/Charles_Louis_Alphonse_Laveran" title="Charles Louis Alphonse Laveran">Charles Louis Alphonse Laveran</a>—a French army doctor working in the military hospital of <a href="/wiki/Constantine,_Algeria" title="Constantine, Algeria">Constantine</a> in <a href="/wiki/Algeria" title="Algeria">Algeria</a>—observed parasites inside the red blood cells of infected people for the first time.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-204"><a href="#cite_note-204">[204]</a></sup> He, therefore, proposed that malaria is caused by this organism, the first time a <a href="/wiki/Protist" title="Protist">protist</a> was identified as causing disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Laveran_bio_205-0"><a href="#cite_note-Laveran_bio-205">[205]</a></sup> For this and later discoveries, he was awarded the 1907 <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_for_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize for Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize for Physiology or Medicine</a>. A year later, <a href="/wiki/Carlos_Finlay" title="Carlos Finlay">Carlos Finlay</a>, a Cuban doctor treating people with <a href="/wiki/Yellow_fever" title="Yellow fever">yellow fever</a> in <a href="/wiki/Havana" title="Havana">Havana</a>, provided strong evidence that mosquitoes were transmitting disease to and from humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tan_2008_206-0"><a href="#cite_note-Tan_2008-206">[206]</a></sup> This work followed earlier suggestions by <a href="/wiki/Josiah_C._Nott" title="Josiah C. Nott">Josiah C. Nott</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chernin_1983_207-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chernin_1983-207">[207]</a></sup> and work by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Patrick_Manson" title="Sir Patrick Manson">Sir Patrick Manson</a>, the "father of tropical medicine", on the transmission of <a href="/wiki/Filariasis" title="Filariasis">filariasis</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Chernin_1977_208-0"><a href="#cite_note-Chernin_1977-208">[208]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Tu_Youyou_5012-1-2015.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2833" data-file-width="2833" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Tu_Youyou_5012-1-2015.jpg/220px-Tu_Youyou_5012-1-2015.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Tu_Youyou_5012-1-2015.jpg/330px-Tu_Youyou_5012-1-2015.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/Tu_Youyou_5012-1-2015.jpg/440px-Tu_Youyou_5012-1-2015.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Tu_Youyou_5012-1-2015.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Chinese medical researcher <a href="/wiki/Tu_Youyou" title="Tu Youyou">Tu Youyou</a> received the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_for_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize for Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize for Physiology or Medicine</a> in 2015 for her work on the antimalarial drug <a href="/wiki/Artemisinin" title="Artemisinin">artemisinin</a>.</div></div></div>
<p>In April 1894, a Scottish physician, <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Ross" title="Ronald Ross">Sir Ronald Ross</a>, visited Sir Patrick Manson at his house on Queen Anne Street, London. This visit was the start of four years of collaboration and fervent research that culminated in 1897 when Ross, who was working in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Presidency_General_Hospital" title="Presidency General Hospital">Presidency General Hospital</a> in <a href="/wiki/Kolkata" title="Kolkata">Calcutta</a>, proved the complete life-cycle of the malaria parasite in mosquitoes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_209-0"><a href="#cite_note-:1-209">[209]</a></sup> He thus proved that the mosquito was the vector for malaria in humans by showing that certain mosquito species transmit malaria to birds. He isolated malaria parasites from the salivary glands of mosquitoes that had fed on infected birds.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_209-1"><a href="#cite_note-:1-209">[209]</a></sup> For this work, Ross received the 1902 Nobel Prize in Medicine. After resigning from the <a href="/wiki/Indian_Medical_Service" title="Indian Medical Service">Indian Medical Service</a>, Ross worked at the newly established <a href="/wiki/Liverpool_School_of_Tropical_Medicine" title="Liverpool School of Tropical Medicine">Liverpool School of Tropical Medicine</a> and directed malaria-control efforts in <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Panama" title="Panama">Panama</a>, <a href="/wiki/Greece" title="Greece">Greece</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mauritius" title="Mauritius">Mauritius</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_Ross_210-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_Ross-210">[210]</a></sup> The findings of Finlay and Ross were later confirmed by a medical board headed by <a href="/wiki/Walter_Reed" title="Walter Reed">Walter Reed</a> in 1900. Its recommendations were implemented by <a href="/wiki/William_C._Gorgas" title="William C. Gorgas">William C. Gorgas</a> in <a href="/wiki/Health_measures_during_the_construction_of_the_Panama_Canal" title="Health measures during the construction of the Panama Canal">the health measures undertaken</a> during construction of the <a href="/wiki/Panama_Canal" title="Panama Canal">Panama Canal</a>. This public-health work saved the lives of thousands of workers and helped develop the methods used in future public-health campaigns against the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Simmons_1979_211-0"><a href="#cite_note-Simmons_1979-211">[211]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 1896, <a href="/wiki/Amico_Bignami" title="Amico Bignami">Amico Bignami</a> discussed the role of mosquitoes in malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-212"><a href="#cite_note-212">[212]</a></sup> In 1898, Bignami, <a href="/wiki/Giovanni_Battista_Grassi" title="Giovanni Battista Grassi">Giovanni Battista Grassi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Giuseppe_Bastianelli" title="Giuseppe Bastianelli">Giuseppe Bastianelli</a> succeeded in showing experimentally the transmission of malaria in humans, using infected mosquitoes to contract malaria themselves which they presented in November 1898 to the <a href="/wiki/Accademia_dei_Lincei" title="Accademia dei Lincei">Accademia dei Lincei</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:1_209-2"><a href="#cite_note-:1-209">[209]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Artemisia_annua_West_Virginia.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="668" data-file-width="1110" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Artemisia_annua_West_Virginia.jpg/220px-Artemisia_annua_West_Virginia.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Artemisia_annua_West_Virginia.jpg/330px-Artemisia_annua_West_Virginia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fd/Artemisia_annua_West_Virginia.jpg/440px-Artemisia_annua_West_Virginia.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Artemisia_annua_West_Virginia.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Artemisia annua</i>, source of the antimalarial drug <a href="/wiki/Artemisinin" title="Artemisinin">artemisinin</a></div></div></div>
<p>The first effective treatment for malaria came from the bark of <a href="/wiki/Cinchona" title="Cinchona">cinchona tree</a>, which contains <a href="/wiki/Quinine" title="Quinine">quinine</a>. This tree grows on the slopes of the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a>, mainly in <a href="/wiki/Peru" title="Peru">Peru</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_peoples_of_Peru" title="Indigenous peoples of Peru">indigenous peoples of Peru</a> made a <a href="/wiki/Tincture" title="Tincture">tincture</a> of cinchona to control fever. Its effectiveness against malaria was found and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jesuit" title="Jesuit">Jesuits</a> introduced the treatment to Europe around 1640; by 1677, it was included in the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/London_Pharmacopoeia" title="London Pharmacopoeia">London Pharmacopoeia</a> as an antimalarial treatment.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kaufman_2005_213-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kaufman_2005-213">[213]</a></sup> It was not until 1820 that the active ingredient, quinine, was extracted from the bark, isolated and named by the French chemists <a href="/wiki/Pierre_Joseph_Pelletier" title="Pierre Joseph Pelletier">Pierre Joseph Pelletier</a> and <a href="/wiki/Joseph_Bienaim%C3%A9_Caventou" title="Joseph Bienaimé Caventou">Joseph Bienaimé Caventou</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Pelletier_1820_214-0"><a href="#cite_note-Pelletier_1820-214">[214]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kyle_1974_215-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kyle_1974-215">[215]</a></sup>
</p><p>Quinine was the predominant malarial medication until the 1920s when other medications began to appear. In the 1940s, chloroquine replaced quinine as the treatment of both uncomplicated and severe malaria until resistance supervened, first in Southeast Asia and South America in the 1950s and then globally in the 1980s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Achan_2011_216-0"><a href="#cite_note-Achan_2011-216">[216]</a></sup>
</p><p>The medicinal value of <i><a href="/wiki/Artemisia_annua" title="Artemisia annua">Artemisia annua</a></i> has been used by Chinese herbalists in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Traditional_Chinese_medicines" title="Traditional Chinese medicines">traditional Chinese medicines</a> for 2,000 years. In 1596, Li Shizhen recommended tea made from qinghao specifically to treat malaria symptoms in his "<a href="/wiki/Compendium_of_Materia_Medica" title="Compendium of Materia Medica">Compendium of Materia Medica</a>". Artemisinins, discovered by Chinese scientist <a href="/wiki/Tu_Youyou" title="Tu Youyou">Tu Youyou</a> and colleagues in the 1970s from the plant <i>Artemisia annua</i>, became the recommended treatment for <i>P. falciparum</i> malaria, administered in severe cases in combination with other antimalarials.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hsu_2006_217-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hsu_2006-217">[217]</a></sup> Tu says she was influenced by a <a href="/wiki/Chinese_herbology" title="Chinese herbology">traditional Chinese herbal medicine</a> source, <i>The Handbook of Prescriptions for Emergency Treatments</i>, written in 340 by <a href="/wiki/Ge_Hong" title="Ge Hong">Ge Hong</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-218"><a href="#cite_note-218">[218]</a></sup> For her work on malaria, <a href="/wiki/Tu_Youyou" title="Tu Youyou">Tu Youyou</a> received the 2015 <a href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Physiology_or_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine">Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nobel_219-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nobel-219">[219]</a></sup>
</p><p><i>Plasmodium vivax</i> was used between 1917 and the 1940s for <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Malariotherapy" title="Malariotherapy">malariotherapy</a>—deliberate injection of malaria parasites to induce a fever to combat certain diseases such as tertiary <a href="/wiki/Syphilis" title="Syphilis">syphilis</a>. In 1927, the inventor of this technique, <a href="/wiki/Julius_Wagner-Jauregg" title="Julius Wagner-Jauregg">Julius Wagner-Jauregg</a>, received the Nobel Prize in Physiology or Medicine for his discoveries. The technique was dangerous, killing about 15% of patients, so it is no longer in use.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vogel_2013_220-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vogel_2013-220">[220]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GuadMarinesMalaria.gif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="379" data-file-width="520" decoding="async" height="160" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/GuadMarinesMalaria.gif/220px-GuadMarinesMalaria.gif" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/GuadMarinesMalaria.gif/330px-GuadMarinesMalaria.gif 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/76/GuadMarinesMalaria.gif/440px-GuadMarinesMalaria.gif 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GuadMarinesMalaria.gif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>U.S. Marines with malaria in a field hospital on <a href="/wiki/Guadalcanal" title="Guadalcanal">Guadalcanal</a>, October 1942</div></div></div>
<p>The first pesticide used for indoor residual spraying was <a href="/wiki/DDT" title="DDT">DDT</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-CDC_history_221-0"><a href="#cite_note-CDC_history-221">[221]</a></sup> Although it was initially used exclusively to combat malaria, its use quickly spread to <a href="/wiki/Agriculture" title="Agriculture">agriculture</a>. In time, pest control, rather than disease control, came to dominate DDT use, and this large-scale agricultural use led to the evolution of <a href="/wiki/Pesticide_resistance" title="Pesticide resistance">pesticide-resistant</a> mosquitoes in many regions. The DDT resistance shown by <i>Anopheles</i> mosquitoes can be compared to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibiotic_resistance" title="Antibiotic resistance">antibiotic resistance</a> shown by bacteria. During the 1960s, awareness of the negative consequences of its indiscriminate use increased, ultimately leading to bans on agricultural applications of DDT in many countries in the 1970s.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-van_den_Berg_2009_85-1"><a href="#cite_note-van_den_Berg_2009-85">[85]</a></sup> Before DDT, malaria was successfully eliminated or controlled in tropical areas like Brazil and Egypt by removing or poisoning the breeding grounds of the mosquitoes or the aquatic habitats of the larval stages, for example by applying the highly toxic arsenic compound <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Paris_Green" title="Paris Green">Paris Green</a> to places with standing water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Killeen_2002_222-0"><a href="#cite_note-Killeen_2002-222">[222]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Malaria_vaccine" title="Malaria vaccine">Malaria vaccines</a> have been an elusive goal of research. The first promising studies demonstrating the potential for a malaria vaccine were performed in 1967 by immunising mice with live, radiation-<a href="/wiki/Attenuator_(genetics)" title="Attenuator (genetics)">attenuated</a> sporozoites, which provided significant protection to the mice upon subsequent injection with normal, viable sporozoites. Since the 1970s, there has been a considerable effort to develop similar vaccination strategies for humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Vanderberg_2009_223-0"><a href="#cite_note-Vanderberg_2009-223">[223]</a></sup> The first vaccine, called <a href="/wiki/RTS,S" title="RTS,S">RTS,S</a>, was approved by European regulators in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walsh2015_224-0"><a href="#cite_note-Walsh2015-224">[224]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Names">Names</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: Names">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Various types of malaria have been called by the names below:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Dorlands_225-0"><a href="#cite_note-Dorlands-225">[225]</a></sup>

</td></tr>
</tbody></table>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Eradication_efforts">Eradication efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Eradication efforts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Members_of_the_Malaria_Commission_on_the_Danube_delta,_1929_Wellcome_L0011626.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1212" data-file-width="1622" decoding="async" height="164" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Members_of_the_Malaria_Commission_on_the_Danube_delta%2C_1929_Wellcome_L0011626.jpg/220px-Members_of_the_Malaria_Commission_on_the_Danube_delta%2C_1929_Wellcome_L0011626.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Members_of_the_Malaria_Commission_on_the_Danube_delta%2C_1929_Wellcome_L0011626.jpg/330px-Members_of_the_Malaria_Commission_on_the_Danube_delta%2C_1929_Wellcome_L0011626.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/97/Members_of_the_Malaria_Commission_on_the_Danube_delta%2C_1929_Wellcome_L0011626.jpg/440px-Members_of_the_Malaria_Commission_on_the_Danube_delta%2C_1929_Wellcome_L0011626.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Members_of_the_Malaria_Commission_on_the_Danube_delta,_1929_Wellcome_L0011626.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Members of the Malaria Commission of the <a href="/wiki/League_of_Nations" title="League of Nations">League of Nations</a> collecting larvae on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Danube_delta" title="Danube delta">Danube delta</a>, 1929</div></div></div>
<p>Malaria has been successfully eliminated or significantly reduced in certain areas, but not globally. Malaria was once common in the United States, but the US eliminated malaria from most parts of the country in the early 20th century using vector control programs, which combined the monitoring and treatment of infected humans, draining of <a href="/wiki/Wetland" title="Wetland">wetland</a> breeding grounds for agriculture and other changes in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Water_management" title="Water management">water management</a> practices, and advances in sanitation, including greater use of glass windows and screens in dwellings.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Meade_2010_226-0"><a href="#cite_note-Meade_2010-226">[226]</a></sup>  The use of the <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticide</a> DDT and other means eliminated malaria from the remaining pockets in southern states of the US the 1950s, as part of the <a href="/wiki/National_Malaria_Eradication_Program" title="National Malaria Eradication Program">National Malaria Eradication Program</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Williams_1963_227-0"><a href="#cite_note-Williams_1963-227">[227]</a></sup>  Most of <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/North_Africa" title="North Africa">North Africa</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Caribbean" title="Caribbean">Caribbean</a>, and parts of <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Asia" title="Asia">Asia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Africa" title="Southern Africa">Southern Africa</a> have also eliminated malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-228"><a href="#cite_note-228">[228]</a></sup> The WHO defines "elimination" (or "malaria-free") as having no domestic transmission (indigenous cases) for the past three years. They also define "pre-elimination" and "elimination" stages when a country has fewer than 5 or 1, respectively, cases per 1000 people at risk per year.
</p><p>
In 1955 the WHO launched the Global Malaria Eradication Program (GMEP), which supported substantial reductions in malaria cases in some countries, including India.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DuintjerTebbensThompson2009_229-0"><a href="#cite_note-DuintjerTebbensThompson2009-229">[229]</a></sup> However, due to vector and parasite resistance and other factors, the <a href="/wiki/Eradication_of_infectious_diseases" title="Eradication of infectious diseases">feasibility of eradicating malaria</a> with the strategy used at the time and resources available led to waning support for the program.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nájera2011_230-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nájera2011-230">[230]</a></sup> WHO suspended the program in 1969.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DuintjerTebbensThompson2009_229-1"><a href="#cite_note-DuintjerTebbensThompson2009-229">[229]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nájera2011_230-1"><a href="#cite_note-Nájera2011-230">[230]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:L0074987_Malaria_eradication_-_the_world_united_against_malaria_(20675407876).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="523" data-file-width="798" decoding="async" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/L0074987_Malaria_eradication_-_the_world_united_against_malaria_%2820675407876%29.jpg/220px-L0074987_Malaria_eradication_-_the_world_united_against_malaria_%2820675407876%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/L0074987_Malaria_eradication_-_the_world_united_against_malaria_%2820675407876%29.jpg/330px-L0074987_Malaria_eradication_-_the_world_united_against_malaria_%2820675407876%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/86/L0074987_Malaria_eradication_-_the_world_united_against_malaria_%2820675407876%29.jpg/440px-L0074987_Malaria_eradication_-_the_world_united_against_malaria_%2820675407876%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:L0074987_Malaria_eradication_-_the_world_united_against_malaria_(20675407876).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>1962 Pakistani postage stamp promoting malaria eradication program</div></div></div>
<p>Target 6C of the <a href="/wiki/Millennium_Development_Goals" title="Millennium Development Goals">Millennium Development Goals</a> included reversal of the global increase in malaria incidence by 2015, with specific targets for children under 5 years old.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi.org_231-0"><a href="#cite_note-doi.org-231">[231]</a></sup>  Since 2000, support for malaria eradication increased, although some actors in the global health community (including voices within the WHO) view malaria eradication as a premature goal and suggest that the establishment of strict deadlines for malaria eradication may be counterproductive as they are likely to be missed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-232"><a href="#cite_note-232">[232]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2006, the organization <a href="/wiki/Malaria_No_More" title="Malaria No More">Malaria No More</a> set a public goal of eliminating malaria from Africa by 2015, and the organization claimed they planned to dissolve if that goal was accomplished. In 2007, <a href="/wiki/World_Malaria_Day" title="World Malaria Day">World Malaria Day</a> was established by the 60th session of <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Assembly" title="World Health Assembly">the World Health Assembly</a>. As of 2018, they are still functioning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Strom_2011_233-0"><a href="#cite_note-Strom_2011-233">[233]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><video class="thumbimage" controls="" data-durationhint="3724" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Malaria_Eradication-_Back_to_the_Future.webm" height="122" id="mwe_player_1" poster="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/12/Malaria_Eradication-_Back_to_the_Future.webm/220px--Malaria_Eradication-_Back_to_the_Future.webm.jpg" preload="none" width="220"><source data-bandwidth="220392" data-framerate="29.917" data-height="356" data-shorttitle="VP9 360P" data-title="VP9 (360P)" data-transcodekey="360p.vp9.webm" data-width="640" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/1/12/Malaria_Eradication-_Back_to_the_Future.webm/Malaria_Eradication-_Back_to_the_Future.webm.360p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm;
