codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/><source data-bandwidth="126024" data-framerate="29.917" data-height="178" data-shorttitle="VP9 180P" data-title="Low bandwidth VP9 (180P)" data-transcodekey="180p.vp9.webm" data-width="320" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/1/12/Malaria_Eradication-_Back_to_the_Future.webm/Malaria_Eradication-_Back_to_the_Future.webm.180p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="143784" data-framerate="29.917" data-height="236" data-shorttitle="VP9 240P" data-title="Small VP9 (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.vp9.webm" data-width="426" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/1/12/Malaria_Eradication-_Back_to_the_Future.webm/Malaria_Eradication-_Back_to_the_Future.webm.240p.vp9.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp9, opus"'/><source data-bandwidth="302024" data-framerate="29.917" data-height="236" data-shorttitle="WebM 240P" data-title="Small WebM (240P)" data-transcodekey="240p.webm" data-width="426" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/transcoded/1/12/Malaria_Eradication-_Back_to_the_Future.webm/Malaria_Eradication-_Back_to_the_Future.webm.240p.webm" type='video/webm; codecs="vp8, vorbis"'/></video> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Malaria_Eradication-_Back_to_the_Future.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Video recording of a set of presentations given in 2010 about humanity's efforts towards malaria eradication</div></div></div>
<p>As of 2012<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit">[update]</a></sup>, <a href="/wiki/The_Global_Fund_to_Fight_AIDS,_Tuberculosis_and_Malaria" title="The Global Fund to Fight AIDS, Tuberculosis and Malaria">The Global Fund to Fight AIDS, Tuberculosis, and Malaria</a> has distributed 230 million insecticide-treated nets intended to stop mosquito-borne transmission of malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Global_Fund_234-0"><a href="#cite_note-Global_Fund-234">[234]</a></sup> The U.S.-based <a href="/wiki/Clinton_Foundation" title="Clinton Foundation">Clinton Foundation</a> has worked to manage demand and stabilize prices in the artemisinin market.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WSJ_2008_235-0"><a href="#cite_note-WSJ_2008-235">[235]</a></sup> Other efforts, such as the Malaria Atlas Project, focus on analysing climate and weather information required to accurately predict malaria spread based on the availability of habitat of malaria-carrying parasites.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Guerra_2007_176-1"><a href="#cite_note-Guerra_2007-176">[176]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Malaria_Policy_Advisory_Committee" title="Malaria Policy Advisory Committee">Malaria Policy Advisory Committee</a> (MPAC) of the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> (WHO) was formed in 2012, "to provide strategic advice and technical input to WHO on all aspects of malaria control and elimination".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-236"><a href="#cite_note-236">[236]</a></sup>  In November 2013, WHO and the malaria vaccine funders group set a goal to develop vaccines designed to interrupt malaria transmission with malaria eradication's long-term goal.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-237"><a href="#cite_note-237">[237]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2015 the WHO targeted a 90% reduction in malaria deaths by 2030,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_238-0"><a href="#cite_note-:0-238">[238]</a></sup> and <a href="/wiki/Bill_Gates" title="Bill Gates">Bill Gates</a> said in 2016 that he thought global eradication would be possible by 2040.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-239"><a href="#cite_note-239">[239]</a></sup> According to the WHO's World Malaria Report 2015, the global mortality rate for malaria fell by 60% between 2000 and 2015. The WHO targeted a further 90% reduction between 2015 and 2030,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-240"><a href="#cite_note-240">[240]</a></sup> with a 40% reduction and eradication in 10 countries by 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-241"><a href="#cite_note-241">[241]</a></sup> However, the 2020 goal was missed with a slight increase in cases compared to 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2020_malaria_report_242-0"><a href="#cite_note-2020_malaria_report-242">[242]</a></sup>
</p><p>Before 2016, the Global Fund against HIV/AIDS, Tuberculosis and Malaria had provided 659 million ITN (insecticide treated bed nets), organise support and education to prevents malaria. The challenges are high due to the lack of funds, the fragile health structure and the remote indigenous population that could be hard to reach and educate. Most of indigenous population rely on self-diagnosis, self-treatment, healer, and traditional medicine. The WHO applied for fund to the Gates Foundation which favour the action of malaria eradication in 2007.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-243"><a href="#cite_note-243">[243]</a></sup> Six countries, the United Arab Emirates, Morocco, Armenia, Turkmenistan, Kyrgyzstan, and Sri Lanka managed to have no endemic cases of  malaria for three consecutive years and certified malaria-free by the WHO despite the stagnation of the funding in 2010.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-doi.org_231-1"><a href="#cite_note-doi.org-231">[231]</a></sup> The funding is essential to finance the cost of medication and hospitalisation cannot be supported by the poor countries where the disease is widely spread. The goal of eradication has not been met nevertheless the decrease rate of the disease is considerable.
</p><p>While 31 out of 92 endemic countries were estimated to be on track with the WHO goals for 2020, 15 countries reported an increase of 40% or more between 2015 and 2020.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2020_malaria_report_242-1"><a href="#cite_note-2020_malaria_report-242">[242]</a></sup> Between 2000 and 30 June 2021, twelve countries were certified by the WHO as being malaria-free.  Argentina and Algeria were declared free of malaria in 2019.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2020_malaria_report_242-2"><a href="#cite_note-2020_malaria_report-242">[242]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-244"><a href="#cite_note-244">[244]</a></sup> El Salvador and China were declared malaria-free in the first half of 2021.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-245"><a href="#cite_note-245">[245]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WMR_2011_246-0"><a href="#cite_note-WMR_2011-246">[246]</a></sup>
</p><p>Regional disparities were evident: <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> was on track to meet WHO's 2020 goals, while Africa, Americas, Eastern Mediterranean and West Pacific regions were off-track.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2020_malaria_report_242-3"><a href="#cite_note-2020_malaria_report-242">[242]</a></sup>  The six <a href="/wiki/Greater_Mekong_Subregion" title="Greater Mekong Subregion">Greater Mekong Subregion</a> countries aim for elimination of <a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_falciparum" title="Plasmodium falciparum"><i>P. falciparum</i></a> transmitted malaria by 2025 and elimination of all malaria by 2030, having achieved a 97% and 90% reduction of cases respectively since 2000.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2020_malaria_report_242-4"><a href="#cite_note-2020_malaria_report-242">[242]</a></sup> Ahead of <a href="/wiki/World_Malaria_Day" title="World Malaria Day">World Malaria Day</a>, 25 April 2021, WHO named 25 countries in which it is working to eliminate malaria by 2025 as part of its E-2025 initiative.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-247"><a href="#cite_note-247">[247]</a></sup>
</p><p>A major challenge to malaria elimination is the persistence of malaria in border regions, making international cooperation crucial.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-248"><a href="#cite_note-248">[248]</a></sup>
</p><p>One of the targets of <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goal_3" title="Sustainable Development Goal 3">Goal 3</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">UN</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Sustainable_Development_Goals" title="Sustainable Development Goals">Sustainable Development Goals</a> is to end the malaria epidemic in all countries by 2030.
</p><p>In 2018, WHO announced that Paraguay was free of malaria, after a national malaria eradication effort that began in 1950.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-249"><a href="#cite_note-249">[249]</a></sup>
</p><p>As of 2019, the eradication process is ongoing, but it will be difficult to achieve a world free of malaria with the current approaches and tools. Only one <a href="/wiki/Malaria_vaccine" title="Malaria vaccine">malaria vaccine</a> is licensed for use, and it shows relatively low effectiveness, while several other vaccine candidates in clinical trials<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-WHO2016_11-3"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2016-11">[11]</a></sup> aim to provide protection for children in endemic areas and reduce the speed of malaria transmission. Approaches may require investing more in research and greater primary health care.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-250"><a href="#cite_note-250">[250]</a></sup> Continuing surveillance will also be important to prevent the return of malaria in countries where the disease has been eliminated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-251"><a href="#cite_note-251">[251]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Society_and_culture">Society and culture</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=27" title="Edit section: Society and culture">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/World_Malaria_Day" title="World Malaria Day">World Malaria Day</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_impact">Economic impact</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=28" title="Edit section: Economic impact">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Saving_Lives_with_SMS_for_Life.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2025" data-file-width="2700" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Saving_Lives_with_SMS_for_Life.jpg/220px-Saving_Lives_with_SMS_for_Life.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Saving_Lives_with_SMS_for_Life.jpg/330px-Saving_Lives_with_SMS_for_Life.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/46/Saving_Lives_with_SMS_for_Life.jpg/440px-Saving_Lives_with_SMS_for_Life.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Saving_Lives_with_SMS_for_Life.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Malaria clinic in Tanzania</div></div></div>
<p>Malaria is not just a disease commonly associated with <a href="/wiki/Poverty" title="Poverty">poverty</a>: some evidence suggests that it is also a cause of poverty and a major hindrance to <a href="/wiki/Economic_development" title="Economic development">economic development</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-IftSoL_17-1"><a href="#cite_note-IftSoL-17">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Worrall_2005_18-1"><a href="#cite_note-Worrall_2005-18">[18]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropical regions</a> are most affected, malaria's furthest influence reaches into some temperate zones that have extreme seasonal changes. The disease has been associated with major negative economic effects on regions where it is widespread. During the late 19th and early 20th centuries, it was a major factor in the slow economic development of the American southern states.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Humphreys_2001_252-0"><a href="#cite_note-Humphreys_2001-252">[252]</a></sup>
</p><p>A comparison of average per capita <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/GDP" title="GDP">GDP</a> in 1995, adjusted for <a href="/wiki/Purchasing_power_parity" title="Purchasing power parity">parity of purchasing power</a>, between countries with malaria and countries without malaria gives a fivefold difference (US$1,526 versus US$8,268). In the period 1965 to 1990, countries where malaria was common had an average per capita GDP that increased only 0.4% per year, compared to 2.4% per year in other countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Sachs_2002_253-0"><a href="#cite_note-Sachs_2002-253">[253]</a></sup>
</p><p>Poverty can increase the risk of malaria since those in poverty do not have the financial capacities to prevent or treat the disease. In its entirety, the economic impact of malaria has been estimated to cost Africa US$12 billion every year. The economic impact includes costs of health care, working days lost due to sickness, days lost in education, decreased productivity due to brain damage from cerebral malaria, and loss of investment and tourism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Greenwood_2005_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Greenwood_2005-19">[19]</a></sup> The disease has a heavy burden in some countries, where it may be responsible for 30–50% of hospital admissions, up to 50% of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Outpatient" title="Outpatient">outpatient</a> visits, and up to 40% of public health spending.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roll_Back_Malaria_WHO_254-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roll_Back_Malaria_WHO-254">[254]</a></sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Malaria_Patient,_Nyangaton,_Ethiopia_(15151075077).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="652" data-file-width="1024" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Malaria_Patient%2C_Nyangaton%2C_Ethiopia_%2815151075077%29.jpg/220px-Malaria_Patient%2C_Nyangaton%2C_Ethiopia_%2815151075077%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Malaria_Patient%2C_Nyangaton%2C_Ethiopia_%2815151075077%29.jpg/330px-Malaria_Patient%2C_Nyangaton%2C_Ethiopia_%2815151075077%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c2/Malaria_Patient%2C_Nyangaton%2C_Ethiopia_%2815151075077%29.jpg/440px-Malaria_Patient%2C_Nyangaton%2C_Ethiopia_%2815151075077%29.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Malaria_Patient,_Nyangaton,_Ethiopia_(15151075077).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Child with malaria in <a href="/wiki/Ethiopia" title="Ethiopia">Ethiopia</a></div></div></div>
<p>Cerebral malaria is one of the leading causes of neurological disabilities in African children.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Idro_2010_169-1"><a href="#cite_note-Idro_2010-169">[169]</a></sup> Studies comparing cognitive functions before and after treatment for severe malarial illness continued to show significantly impaired school performance and cognitive abilities even after recovery.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Fernando_2010_167-3"><a href="#cite_note-Fernando_2010-167">[167]</a></sup> Consequently, severe and cerebral malaria have far-reaching <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Socioeconomic" title="Socioeconomic">socioeconomic</a> consequences that extend beyond the immediate effects of the disease.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ricci_2012_255-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ricci_2012-255">[255]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Counterfeit_and_substandard_drugs">Counterfeit and substandard drugs</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=29" title="Edit section: Counterfeit and substandard drugs">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sophisticated <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Counterfeit_drugs" title="Counterfeit drugs">counterfeits</a> have been found in several Asian countries such as <a href="/wiki/Cambodia" title="Cambodia">Cambodia</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Lon_2006_256-0"><a href="#cite_note-Lon_2006-256">[256]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newton_2008_257-0"><a href="#cite_note-Newton_2008-257">[257]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Indonesia" title="Indonesia">Indonesia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Laos" title="Laos">Laos</a>, <a href="/wiki/Thailand" title="Thailand">Thailand</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Vietnam" title="Vietnam">Vietnam</a>, and are a major cause of avoidable death in those countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Newton_2006_258-0"><a href="#cite_note-Newton_2006-258">[258]</a></sup> The WHO said that studies indicate that up to 40% of artesunate-based malaria medications are counterfeit, especially in the Greater <a href="/wiki/Mekong" title="Mekong">Mekong</a> region. They have established a rapid alert system to rapidly report information about counterfeit drugs to relevant authorities in participating countries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Parry_2005_259-0"><a href="#cite_note-Parry_2005-259">[259]</a></sup> There is no reliable way for doctors or lay people to detect counterfeit drugs without help from a laboratory. Companies are attempting to combat the persistence of counterfeit drugs by using new technology to provide security from source to distribution.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gautam_2009_260-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gautam_2009-260">[260]</a></sup>
</p><p>Another clinical and public health concern is the proliferation of substandard antimalarial medicines resulting from inappropriate concentration of ingredients, contamination with other drugs or toxic impurities, poor quality ingredients, poor stability and inadequate packaging.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Caudron_2008_261-0"><a href="#cite_note-Caudron_2008-261">[261]</a></sup> A 2012 study demonstrated that roughly one-third of antimalarial medications in Southeast Asia and Sub-Saharan Africa failed chemical analysis, packaging analysis, or were falsified.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Nayyar_2012_262-0"><a href="#cite_note-Nayyar_2012-262">[262]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="War">War</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=30" title="Edit section: War">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:%22Don%27t_go_to_Bed_with_Malaria_Mosquito%22_-_NARA_-_514146.tif"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3000" data-file-width="2466" decoding="async" height="268" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/%22Don%27t_go_to_Bed_with_Malaria_Mosquito%22_-_NARA_-_514146.tif/lossy-page1-220px-%22Don%27t_go_to_Bed_with_Malaria_Mosquito%22_-_NARA_-_514146.tif.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/%22Don%27t_go_to_Bed_with_Malaria_Mosquito%22_-_NARA_-_514146.tif/lossy-page1-330px-%22Don%27t_go_to_Bed_with_Malaria_Mosquito%22_-_NARA_-_514146.tif.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/%22Don%27t_go_to_Bed_with_Malaria_Mosquito%22_-_NARA_-_514146.tif/lossy-page1-440px-%22Don%27t_go_to_Bed_with_Malaria_Mosquito%22_-_NARA_-_514146.tif.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:%22Don%27t_go_to_Bed_with_Malaria_Mosquito%22_-_NARA_-_514146.tif" title="Enlarge"></a></div>World War II poster</div></div></div>
<p>Throughout history, the contraction of malaria has played a prominent role in the fates of government rulers, nation-states, military personnel, and military actions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Russell_2009_263-0"><a href="#cite_note-Russell_2009-263">[263]</a></sup> In 1910, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nobel_Prize_in_Medicine" title="Nobel Prize in Medicine">Nobel Prize in Medicine</a>-winner Ronald Ross (himself a malaria survivor), published a book titled <i>The Prevention of Malaria</i> that included a chapter titled "The Prevention of Malaria in War". The chapter's author, Colonel C. H. Melville, Professor of Hygiene at <a href="/wiki/Royal_Army_Medical_College" title="Royal Army Medical College">Royal Army Medical College</a> in London, addressed the prominent role that malaria has historically played during wars: "The history of malaria in war might almost be taken to be the history of war itself, certainly the history of war in the Christian era. ... It is probably the case that many of the so-called camp fevers, and probably also a considerable proportion of the camp dysentery, of the wars of the sixteenth, seventeenth and eighteenth centuries were malarial in origin."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ross_1910_264-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ross_1910-264">[264]</a></sup> In British-occupied India the cocktail <a href="/wiki/Gin_and_tonic#History" title="Gin and tonic">gin and tonic</a> may have come about as a way of taking quinine, known for its antimalarial properties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-265"><a href="#cite_note-265">[265]</a></sup>
</p><p>Malaria was the most significant health hazard encountered by U.S. troops in the South Pacific during <a href="/wiki/World_War_II" title="World War II">World War II</a>, where about 500,000 men were infected.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Bray_2004_266-0"><a href="#cite_note-Bray_2004-266">[266]</a></sup> According to Joseph Patrick Byrne, "Sixty thousand American soldiers died of malaria during the African and South Pacific campaigns."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Byrne_2008_267-0"><a href="#cite_note-Byrne_2008-267">[267]</a></sup>
</p><p>Significant financial investments have been made to procure existing and create new antimalarial agents. During <a href="/wiki/World_War_I" title="World War I">World War I</a> and World War II, inconsistent supplies of the natural antimalaria drugs <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cinchona_bark" title="Cinchona bark">cinchona bark</a> and quinine prompted substantial funding into <a href="/wiki/Research_and_development" title="Research and development">research and development</a> of other drugs and vaccines. American military organisations conducting such research initiatives include the Navy Medical Research Center, <a href="/wiki/Walter_Reed_Army_Institute_of_Research" title="Walter Reed Army Institute of Research">Walter Reed Army Institute of Research</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/U.S._Army_Medical_Research_Institute_of_Infectious_Diseases" title="U.S. Army Medical Research Institute of Infectious Diseases">U.S. Army Medical Research Institute of Infectious Diseases</a> of the US Armed Forces.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-malariasite1_268-0"><a href="#cite_note-malariasite1-268">[268]</a></sup>
</p><p>Additionally, initiatives have been founded such as Malaria Control in War Areas (MCWA), established in 1942, and its successor, the Communicable Disease Center (now known as the <a href="/wiki/Centers_for_Disease_Control_and_Prevention" title="Centers for Disease Control and Prevention">Centers for Disease Control and Prevention</a>, or CDC) established in 1946. According to the CDC, MCWA "was established to control malaria around military training bases in the southern United States and its territories, where malaria was still problematic".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated1_269-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated1-269">[269]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Research">Research</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=31" title="Edit section: Research">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The Malaria Eradication Research Agenda (malERA) initiative was a consultative process to identify which areas of research and development (R&amp;D) must be addressed for worldwide eradication of malaria.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-270"><a href="#cite_note-270">[270]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-271"><a href="#cite_note-271">[271]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Vaccine">Vaccine</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=32" title="Edit section: Vaccine">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Malaria_vaccine" title="Malaria vaccine">Malaria vaccine</a></div>
<p>A vaccine against malaria called <a href="/wiki/RTS,S" title="RTS,S">RTS,S/AS01 (RTS,S)</a> was approved by European regulators in 2015.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Walsh2015_224-1"><a href="#cite_note-Walsh2015-224">[224]</a></sup> As of 2019 it is undergoing pilot trials in 3 sub-Saharan African countries – Ghana, Kenya and Malawi – as part of the WHO's Malaria Vaccine Implementation Programme (MVIP).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-272"><a href="#cite_note-272">[272]</a></sup>
</p><p>Immunity (or, more accurately, <a href="/wiki/Immune_tolerance" title="Immune tolerance">tolerance</a>) to <i>P. falciparum</i> malaria does occur naturally, but only in response to years of repeated infection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Tran_2012_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-Tran_2012-50">[50]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-273"><a href="#cite_note-273">[273]</a></sup> An individual can be protected from a <i>P. falciparum</i> infection if they receive about a thousand bites from mosquitoes that carry a version of the parasite rendered non-infective by a dose of <a href="/wiki/X-ray" title="X-ray">X-ray</a> <a href="/wiki/Irradiation" title="Irradiation">irradiation</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hill_2011_274-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hill_2011-274">[274]</a></sup> The highly <a href="/wiki/Polymorphism_(biology)" title="Polymorphism (biology)">polymorphic</a> nature of many <i>P. falciparum</i> proteins results in significant challenges to vaccine design. Vaccine candidates that target antigens on gametes, zygotes, or ookinetes in the mosquito midgut aim to block the transmission of malaria. These transmission-blocking vaccines induce <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antibodies" title="Antibodies">antibodies</a> in the human blood; when a mosquito takes a blood meal from a protected individual, these antibodies prevent the parasite from completing its development in the mosquito.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Cromptom_2010_275-0"><a href="#cite_note-Cromptom_2010-275">[275]</a></sup> Other vaccine candidates, targeting the blood-stage of the parasite's life cycle, have been inadequate on their own.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graves_2006b_276-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graves_2006b-276">[276]</a></sup> For example, <a href="/wiki/Manuel_Elkin_Patarroyo" title="Manuel Elkin Patarroyo">SPf66</a> was tested extensively in areas where the disease was common in the 1990s, but trials showed it to be insufficiently effective.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Graves_2006_277-0"><a href="#cite_note-Graves_2006-277">[277]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 2021, researchers from the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Oxford" title="University of Oxford">University of Oxford</a> reported findings from a <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_clinical_research" title="Phases of clinical research">Phase IIb trial</a> of a candidate malaria vaccine, R21/Matrix-M, which demonstrated efficacy of 77% over 12-months of follow-up. This vaccine is the first to meet the <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a>'s Malaria Vaccine Technology Roadmap goal of a vaccine with at least 75% efficacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-278"><a href="#cite_note-278">[278]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Medications_2">Medications</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=33" title="Edit section: Medications">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Malaria parasites contain <a href="/wiki/Apicoplast" title="Apicoplast">apicoplasts</a>, organelles related to the <a href="/wiki/Plastid" title="Plastid">plastids</a> found in plants, complete with their own <a href="/wiki/Genome" title="Genome">genomes</a>. These apicoplasts are thought to have originated through the <a href="/wiki/Endosymbiont" title="Endosymbiont">endosymbiosis</a> of algae and play a crucial role in various aspects of parasite <a href="/wiki/Metabolism" title="Metabolism">metabolism</a>, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Fatty_acid_biosynthesis" title="Fatty acid biosynthesis">fatty acid biosynthesis</a>. Over 400 proteins have been found to be produced by apicoplasts and these are now being investigated as possible targets for novel antimalarial drugs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Kalanon_2010_279-0"><a href="#cite_note-Kalanon_2010-279">[279]</a></sup>
</p><p>With the onset of drug-resistant <i>Plasmodium</i> parasites, new strategies are being developed to combat the widespread disease. One such approach lies in the introduction of synthetic <a href="/wiki/Pyridoxal" title="Pyridoxal">pyridoxal</a>-amino acid <a href="/wiki/Adduct" title="Adduct">adducts</a>, which are taken up by the parasite and ultimately interfere with its ability to create several essential <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/B_vitamin" title="B vitamin">B vitamins</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Müller_2010_280-0"><a href="#cite_note-Müller_2010-280">[280]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Du_2011_281-0"><a href="#cite_note-Du_2011-281">[281]</a></sup> Antimalarial drugs using <a href="/wiki/Organometallic_chemistry" title="Organometallic chemistry">synthetic metal-based</a> <a href="/wiki/Coordination_complex" title="Coordination complex">complexes</a> are attracting research interest.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Biot_2012_282-0"><a href="#cite_note-Biot_2012-282">[282]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Roux_2012_283-0"><a href="#cite_note-Roux_2012-283">[283]</a></sup>
</p>
<ul><li>(+)-SJ733: Part of a wider class of experimental drugs called <a href="/wiki/Spiroindolone" title="Spiroindolone">spiroindolone</a>. It inhibits the ATP4 protein of infected red blood cells that cause the cells to shrink and become rigid like the aging cells. This triggers the immune system to eliminate the infected cells from the system as demonstrated in a mouse model. As of 2014, a <a href="/wiki/Phases_of_clinical_research#Phase_1" title="Phases of clinical research">Phase 1 clinical trial</a> to assess the safety profile in human is planned by the <a href="/wiki/Howard_Hughes_Medical_Institute" title="Howard Hughes Medical Institute">Howard Hughes Medical Institute</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atp4_284-0"><a href="#cite_note-atp4-284">[284]</a></sup></li>
<li>NITD246 and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/NITD609" title="NITD609">NITD609</a>: Also belonged to the class of spiroindolone and target the ATP4 protein.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-atp4_284-1"><a href="#cite_note-atp4-284">[284]</a></sup></li></ul>
<p>On the basis of molecular docking outcomes, compounds 3j, 4b, 4h, 4m were exhibited selectivity towards PfLDH. The post docking analysis displayed stable dynamic behavior of all the selected compounds compared to Chloroquine. The end state thermodynamics analysis stated 3j compound as a selective and potent PfLDH inhibitor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-pmid31903852_285-0"><a href="#cite_note-pmid31903852-285">[285]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="New_targets">New targets</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=34" title="Edit section: New targets">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Targeting <i>Plasmodium</i> liver-stage parasites selectively is emerging as an alternative strategy in the face of resistance to the latest frontline combination therapies against blood stages of the parasite.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanway2019_286-0"><a href="#cite_note-Stanway2019-286">[286]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a research conducted in 2019, using experimental analysis with knockout (KO) mutants of <i>Plasmodium berguei</i> the authors were able to identify genes that are potentially essential in the liver stage. Moreover, they generated a computational model to analyse pre–erytrocytic development and liver–stage metabolism. Combining both methods they identified seven metabolic subsystems that become essential compared to the blood stage. Some of these metabolic pathways are fatty acid synthesis and elongation, tricarboxylic acid, amino acid and heme metabolism among others.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanway2019_286-1"><a href="#cite_note-Stanway2019-286">[286]</a></sup>
</p><p>Specifically, they studied 3 subsystems: fatty acid synthesis and elongation, and amino sugar biosynthesis. For the first two pathways they demonstrated a clear dependence of the liver stage on its own fatty acid metabolism.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanway2019_286-2"><a href="#cite_note-Stanway2019-286">[286]</a></sup>
</p><p>They proved for the first time the critical role of amino sugar biosynthesis in the liver stage of <i>P. berghei</i>. The uptake of N–acetyl–glucosamine appears to be limited in the liver stage, being its synthesis needed for the parasite development.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanway2019_286-3"><a href="#cite_note-Stanway2019-286">[286]</a></sup>
</p><p>These findings and the computational model provide a basis for the design of antimalarial therapies targeting metabolic proteins.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Stanway2019_286-4"><a href="#cite_note-Stanway2019-286">[286]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-287"><a href="#cite_note-287">[287]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Other">Other</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=35" title="Edit section: Other">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A non-chemical vector control strategy involves genetic manipulation of malaria mosquitoes. Advances in <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a> technologies make it possible to introduce foreign DNA into the mosquito genome and either decrease the lifespan of the mosquito, or make it more resistant to the malaria parasite. <a href="/wiki/Sterile_insect_technique" title="Sterile insect technique">Sterile insect technique</a> is a genetic control method whereby large numbers of sterile male mosquitoes are reared and released. Mating with wild females reduces the wild population in the subsequent generation; repeated releases eventually eliminate the target population.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Raghavendra_2011_74-1"><a href="#cite_note-Raghavendra_2011-74">[74]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Genomics" title="Genomics">Genomics</a> is central to malaria research. With the <a href="/wiki/Whole_genome_sequencing" title="Whole genome sequencing">sequencing</a> of <i>P. falciparum</i>, one of its vectors <i>Anopheles gambiae</i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Human_genome" title="Human genome">human genome</a>, the genetics of all three organisms in the malaria life cycle can be studied.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Aultman_2002_288-0"><a href="#cite_note-Aultman_2002-288">[288]</a></sup> Another new application of genetic technology is the ability to produce <a href="/wiki/Genetically_modified_organism" title="Genetically modified organism">genetically modified</a> mosquitoes that do not transmit malaria, potentially allowing <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Biological_control" title="Biological control">biological control</a> of malaria transmission.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ito_2002_289-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ito_2002-289">[289]</a></sup>
</p><p>In one study, a genetically modified strain of <i><a href="/wiki/Anopheles_stephensi" title="Anopheles stephensi">Anopheles stephensi</a></i> was created that no longer supported malaria transmission, and this resistance was passed down to mosquito offspring.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-290"><a href="#cite_note-290">[290]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Gene_drive" title="Gene drive">Gene drive</a> is a technique for changing wild populations, for instance to combat or eliminate insects so they cannot transmit diseases (in particular mosquitoes in the cases of malaria,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ImperialCollegeLondon_291-0"><a href="#cite_note-ImperialCollegeLondon-291">[291]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Zika_virus" title="Zika virus">zika</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BloombergFlam_292-0"><a href="#cite_note-BloombergFlam-292">[292]</a></sup> dengue and yellow fever).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-:0_238-1"><a href="#cite_note-:0-238">[238]</a></sup>
</p><p>In December 2020, a review article found that malaria-endemic regions had lower reported <a href="/wiki/COVID-19" title="COVID-19">COVID-19</a> case fatality rates on average than regions where malaria was not known to be endemic.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-293"><a href="#cite_note-293">[293]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Other_animals">Other animals</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=36" title="Edit section: Other animals">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>While there are no <a href="/wiki/Zoonosis" title="Zoonosis">animal reservoirs</a> for the strains of malaria that cause human infections,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-294"><a href="#cite_note-294">[294]</a></sup> nearly 200 parasitic <i>Plasmodium</i> species have been identified that infect <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasmodium_species_infecting_birds" title="Plasmodium species infecting birds">birds</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasmodium_species_infecting_reptiles" title="Plasmodium species infecting reptiles">reptiles</a>, and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Plasmodium_species_infecting_mammals_other_than_primates" title="Plasmodium species infecting mammals other than primates">other mammals</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rich_2006_295-0"><a href="#cite_note-Rich_2006-295">[295]</a></sup> and about 30 species naturally infect non-human primates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Baird_2009_296-0"><a href="#cite_note-Baird_2009-296">[296]</a></sup> Some malaria parasites that affect non-human primates (NHP) serve as <a href="/wiki/Model_organism" title="Model organism">model organisms</a> for human malarial parasites, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_coatneyi" title="Plasmodium coatneyi">P. coatneyi</a></i> (a model for <i>P. falciparum</i>) and <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_cynomolgi" title="Plasmodium cynomolgi">P. cynomolgi</a></i> (<i>P. vivax</i>). Diagnostic techniques used to detect parasites in NHP are similar to those employed for humans.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Ameri_2010_297-0"><a href="#cite_note-Ameri_2010-297">[297]</a></sup> Malaria parasites that infect rodents are widely used as models in research, such as <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_berghei" title="Plasmodium berghei">P. berghei</a></i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Mlambo_2008_298-0"><a href="#cite_note-Mlambo_2008-298">[298]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Avian_malaria" title="Avian malaria">Avian malaria</a> primarily affects species of the order <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Passeriformes" title="Passeriformes">Passeriformes</a>, and poses a substantial threat to birds of <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Galapagos" title="Galapagos">Galapagos</a>, and other <a href="/wiki/Archipelago" title="Archipelago">archipelagoes</a>. The parasite <i><a href="/wiki/Plasmodium_relictum" title="Plasmodium relictum">P. relictum</a></i> is known to play a role in limiting the distribution and abundance of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Endemic_birds_of_Hawaii" title="Endemic birds of Hawaii">endemic Hawaiian birds</a>. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Global warming</a> is expected to increase the prevalence and global distribution of <a href="/wiki/Avian_malaria" title="Avian malaria">avian malaria</a>, as elevated temperatures provide optimal conditions for parasite reproduction.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-LaPointe_2012_299-0"><a href="#cite_note-LaPointe_2012-299">[299]</a></sup>
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Malaria&amp;action=edit&amp;section=37" title="Edit section: References">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
